The Attributes of Meaning (al-Ṣifāt al-Maʿnawiyya)
Sanusi defines and enumerates the seven attributes of meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attributes of meaning are the necessary consequential attributes of the divine essence that follow from the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī).

Core Idea
The attributes of meaning are the 'so what?' of God's attributes — they describe what God necessarily is given His perfections.

Sanusi now introduces the fourth category of divine attributes: the attributes of meaning. These are not additional attributes separate from the attributes of meanings (like power, will, knowledge) but rather the necessary descriptions that follow from them. If God has knowledge, He is knowing; if He has power, He is powerful. This passage defines and enumerates them, grounding the Ashʿarī position that God's essence is described by these seven necessary descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the category
The passage states these are the fourth category of divine attributes. They are also called intellectual attributes (iʿtibāriyya) or states (aḥwāl) according to the theory that affirms them. Know that they are attributes of the self (ṣifāt al-dhāt) necessary for (lāzima) the attributes of meanings.
2
Memorize the enumeration
There are seven attributes: powerful (qādir), willing (murīd), knowing (ʿālim), living (ḥayy), hearing (samīʿ), seeing (baṣīr), and speaking (mutakallim). Note that they correspond exactly to the seven attributes of meanings.
3
Avoid the confusion
The common misunderstanding is that these are separate attributes. In fact, they are necessary consequences (lāzima) of the attributes of meanings, not independent entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفات المعنوية ( الاعتبارية ) ، أو هي ( الأحوال ) على القول بها الرابع : الصفاتُ المعنويَّةُ : وهي صفات الذات اللازمة لصفاتِ المعاني ؛ وهي : كونه تعالى قادراً ، ومريداً ، وعالماً ، وحيّاً ، وسميعاً ، وبصيراً ، ومُتكلّماً
The attributes of meaning (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyya) (the intellectual ones) or they are (the states) according to the theory that affirms them. The fourth: the attributes of meaning: they are attributes of the self necessary for the attributes of meanings; and they are: His being, exalted, powerful (qādir), willing (murīd), knowing (‘ālim), living (ḥayy), hearing (samī‘), seeing (baṣīr), and speaking (mutakallim).
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage classifies the fourth category of divine attributes: the attributes of meaning (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyya). These are also called intellectual attributes (i‘tibāriyya) or states (aḥwāl) according to the theory that affirms states. The author defines them as attributes of the divine self that are necessarily concomitant with the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-ma‘ānī). That is, once we affirm that God has power (qudra), will (irāda), knowledge (‘ilm), life (ḥayāt), hearing (sam‘), sight (baṣar), and speech (kalām), this necessarily implies that God is powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, and speaking. These are the seven attributes of meaning corresponding to the seven attributes of meanings. Note: The enumeration lists seven attributes: قادر, مريد, عالم, حي, سميع, بصير, متكلم. This is a standard Ash‘ari doctrine.

الصفات الاعتبارية
الأحوال
القادرية
المريدية
العالمية
الحياة
السمعية
البصرية
الكلامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These are separate independent attributes alongside the attributes of meanings.
Correction: They are necessary consequences (lāzima) of the attributes of meanings, not separate entities. Once God has power, He is powerful.

Checkpoint
How are al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya related to al-ṣifāt al-maʿānī?
A) They are the same as the attributes of meanings.
B) They are necessary consequences (lāzima) of the attributes of meanings.
C) They are attributes of actions.
D) They are negations.

Key Takeaway
The attributes of meaning are the seven necessary descriptions of God's essence that follow from His seven attributes of meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is necessarily powerful (qādir), why do you hesitate to rely on His power in your current difficulty? Are you acting as if His power is not real?
?
If God is necessarily knowing (ʿālim) of everything, including your secret thoughts, what thought do you need to repent from right now because you forgot He sees it?
?
The attributes of meaning are necessary descriptions of God. Does your belief in these attributes affect how you speak about God? Do you ever describe God in ways that contradict these necessary attributes, even unintentionally?

Attributes of Actions and Their Two Types
Sanusi defines the fifth category, attributes of actions, and divides it into two types: existential and privative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of attributes of actions (ṣifāt al-af‘āl) and their division into existential and privative types.

Core Idea
God's actions are relational, not eternal; they include both bringing into existence and withholding punishment.

Sanusi introduces a fifth category of divine attributes added by some scholars: attributes of actions. These describe God's actual connection with creation through power and will. The passage then divides them into two types, illustrating with examples including forgiveness as a privative act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Let the author's own wording introduce the concept of attributes of actions.
Notice the phrase 'al-ta‘alluq al-tanjīzī' – actual connection.
2
Identify the two types of attributes of actions
After defining the category, Sanusi divides it into two types: existential and privative. Find these in the passage and note the examples given for each.
Forgiveness is an example of the privative type.
3
Understand the nuance in the privative type
The passage discusses a theological dispute about whether omission (tark) counts as an act. Note how this affects the classification.
This shows the complexity of ascribing actions to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزادَ بعضُهم قسماً خامساً : وهو صفات الأفعال : وهي عنِ التعلَّقِ التنجيزي للقدرة والإرادة بالممكناتِ ؛ كخلقِهِ تعالى ورَزْقِهِ وإماتته وتحريكه وتسكينه ، وإن شئت قلت : هي عبارة عن صدورِ الممكناتِ عن القدرةِ والإرادة ؛ وهي تنقسم إلى قسمين : صفةٌ فعليَّةٌ وجودية : كالأمثلة المذكورة . وصفة فعلية سلبية : كعفوه تعالى عمَّنْ شاءَ مِنْ أهل المعاصي ؛ فإنَّهُ عبارةٌ عن تركِ العقوبة لمَنْ يستحقُها ، ولا شك أنَّ هذا الترك مُتأخّرُ عن المعصية الحادثة ، وهو فعل بناءً على أنَّ الترك فعل ، أو سلبُ فعلِ العقوبة لمستحقها بناءً على أنَّهُ ليس بفعل .
And some added a fifth division: the attributes of actions (ṣifāt al-af‘āl): they concern the actual connection (al-ta‘alluq al-tanjīzī) of power and will with possibles; such as His, exalted, creating, providing, causing death, moving, and stilling. And if you wish you may say: they are the expression of the occurrence of possibles from power and will. And they are divided into two types: an existential attribute of action (ṣifa fi‘liyya wujūdiyya), like the mentioned examples; and a privative attribute of action (ṣifa fi‘liyya salbiyya), like His, exalted, forgiving whom He wills of the people of sins; for it is an expression of the omission of punishment from one who deserves it, and there is no doubt that this omission occurs after the occurrence of the sin, and it is an act based on the view that omission is an act, or the negation of the act of punishment from the one who deserves it based on the view that it is not an act.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth category of divine attributes added by some scholars: the attributes of actions (ṣifāt al-af‘āl), also referred to as the attribute of ‘takwīn’ (bringing into existence) favored by the Māturīdīs. The author defines them as the actual (or existential) connection (al-ta‘alluq al-tanjīzī) of God's power and will with created things (al-mumkināt). They are not eternal attributes subsisting in God's essence but rather relational descriptions. Examples include creating, providing, causing death, moving, and stilling. Alternatively, they can be described as the occurrence of possibles from power and will. This category is then divided into two subtypes: (1) existential attribute of action, such as the examples given; (2) privative attribute of action, such as God's forgiveness (al-‘afw), which is defined as the omission of punishment from one who deserves it. The author notes that this omission occurs after the sin, and there is a theological dispute about whether omission (tark) is an act or not. If it is considered an act, then forgiveness is an attribute of action; if not, it is the negation of an act. This highlights the complexity of ascribing actions to God.

Key Terms
صفات الأفعال
التعلق التنجيزي
التكوين
صفة فعلية وجودية
صفة فعلية سلبية
العفو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes of actions are eternal attributes subsisting in God's essence.
Correction: They are relational descriptions that occur when God creates; they are not eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is God's forgiveness (al-‘afw) understood?
A) As an eternal attribute of essence.
B) As the creation of mercy.
C) As the omission of punishment from one who deserves it.
D) As the absence of will to punish.

Key Takeaway
Attributes of actions are relational descriptions of God's connection with creation, divided into existential and privative types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that God's forgiveness is not a positive act but an omission of punishment that you deserve. Does this humble you or make you take His mercy for granted? Ponder how you can cultivate a deeper gratitude for this 'omission' today.
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that God's forgiveness is a real action that occurs after your sin, as the passage states? How does this temporal reality affect the urgency with which you repent? Write one specific change you will make in your repentance habits.
?
If attributes of actions are relational and occur in time, how does that shape your understanding of God's involvement in your daily life? Are you living as if God's actions toward you are immediate and personal? Identify one area where you can actively trust in God's active (or privative) attributes this week.

The Comprehensive Attributes: Divinity, Majesty, Grandeur
Sanusi presents a sixth category: comprehensive attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of comprehensive attributes as a sixth category that unites all other divine attributes.

Core Idea
Comprehensive attributes are holistic descriptions implying all perfections.

In this short passage, Sanusi presents a sixth category of divine attributes — one that some scholars added. These are not separate attributes like existence or power, but rather comprehensive descriptions like 'divinity' that encompass all other attributes. Let's explore what this means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's wording sink in.
Notice the phrase 'جامعة لسائر أقسام الصفات' — it shows the key idea.
2
Identify what makes them comprehensive
Ask yourself: why are attributes like divinity called 'comprehensive'? The passage says they unite all other divisions of attributes.
They are not additional individual attributes but overarching descriptions.
3
Consider why some scholars added this category
Reflect on the phrase 'وزاد بعضهم' — some scholars added this sixth category. This indicates it is not universally agreed upon.
The standard five categories are well-known; this is an extension.
4
Reflect on the examples given
The examples are al-ulūhiyya (divinity), al-kibriyā’ (majesty), and al-‘aẓama (grandeur). Think about how each implies all attributes of perfection.
For instance, 'divinity' implies existence, power, knowledge, etc.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you grasped the characteristic of comprehensive attributes.
The question is based strictly on the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزاد بعضُهم قسماً سادساً : وهو الصفاتُ الجامعة لسائر أقسام الصفاتِ ؛ كالأُلوهية والكبرياء والعظمة
And some added a sixth division: the comprehensive attributes (al-ṣifāt al-jāmi‘a) that unite all other divisions of attributes; such as divinity (al-ulūhiyya), majesty (al-kibriyā’), and grandeur (al-‘aẓama).
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage adds a sixth category of divine attributes: the comprehensive attributes (al-ṣifāt al-jāmi‘a). These are attributes that encompass all other categories of attributes—both attributes of essence and attributes of action. Examples given are al-ulūhiyya (divinity), al-kibriyā’ (majesty), and al-‘aẓama (grandeur). These attributes are not additional separate entities but rather holistic descriptions that imply all perfections.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that comprehensive attributes are completely separate attributes like existence or power.
Correction: Comprehensive attributes are not separate individual attributes; they are holistic descriptions that encompass all other attributes. For example, 'divinity' implies all perfections of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Majesty (al-kibriyā’) implies that Allah is utterly beyond your comprehension. In what area of your life are you still trying to 'tame' Allah by reducing Him to something manageable? What would change if you let al-kibriyā’ dominate your thoughts today?
?
Grandeur (al-‘aẓama) means that every act of worship is dwarfed by His greatness. Do you ever pray or obey feeling that your actions are too small to matter? How can the reality of al-‘aẓama free you from despair or arrogance?

Focus on Attributes of Meanings and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Sanusi explains his focus on attributes of meanings and refutes Mu'tazilite denials.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi prioritized the attributes of meanings and how his arguments refute the Mu'tazilite positions on speech and will.

Core Idea
Eternal attributes are essential; Mu'tazilite alternatives lead to contradictions.

This passage reveals why the author dedicated his preliminaries to defending the attributes of meanings. The Mu'tazila reduce most attributes to actions or negations, and their own positions on speech and will expose internal inconsistencies that the Ash'arī argument exploits.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the author's tone—he is concluding his classification and setting up a refutation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite positions
Find the three distinct Mu'tazilite views mentioned: (1) speech as a created attribute of action, (2) will as an originated accident subsisting by itself, (3) will as a negative attribute (denied entirely).
3
Understand the critique of originated will
The author argues that if will is originated, it implies renewal (tajaddud), which would require a change in God's attribute of willing (murīdiyya). Since the Mu'tazila affirm states (aḥwāl), this would force them to accept originated states for the Pre-Eternal, which is impossible.
This is the core logical refutation.
4
Reflect on the result
Note that some Mu'tazila (al-Ka'bī and al-Najjār) avoided the contradiction by denying will altogether, reducing it to a negative attribute. This shows the extreme consequence of their premises.
Contrast with the Ash'arī position of affirming eternal attributes of meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنَّما تعرَّضنا في هذه المقدمات لبيان قسم واحدٍ ؛ وهي المعاني ؛ اعتناء بثبوتها ، وإشارةً إلى وجوب وجودها ؛ ردّاً على المعتزلة الذين قالوا بنفيها ولم يثبتوا منها إلا الكلام ، وجعلوه صفةً فعلية بناءً منهم على حصّرِ الكلام في الحروفِ والأصوات ، فمعنى كونه تعالى مُتكلّماً عندهم : أنَّهُ فَاعل للكلام ، خالق له في محل ، وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى الردُّ عليهم عند تعرضنا لشرح الكلامِ القديم وأثبت أيضاً معتزلة البصرة : الإرادةَ ، إلا أنَّهم جعلوها صفةً حادثةً قائمة بنفسها ، لا في محل ولم يتنبهوا أنَّ حدوث الإرادة دال على التجدُّد ، وعليه : يلزمهم تجدد المريدية لأنهم يثبتون الأحوال ، وتجدد المعنوية دال على الحدوث قطعاً ، فيلزمهم إثبات أحوال حادثة للقديم سبحانه ، ولهذا ذهب الكعبي والنجار مِنَ المعتزلة إلى إنكار هذه الصفة أيضاً، وجعلوها كصفات السلوب ؛ بمعنى : أنه تعالى ليس بمستكره ولا مغلوب !
And we have only addressed in these preliminaries the exposition of one category—the attributes of meanings—out of concern for their establishment and as an indication of their necessary existence, as a refutation of the Mu‘tazila who said to deny them and did not affirm among them except speech, and they made it an attribute of action based on their restricting speech to letters and sounds. So the meaning of His being, exalted, speaking according to them is that He is the doer of speech, the creator of it in a locus. And the refutation of them will come, if God wills, when we treat the explanation of eternal speech. And also the Mu‘tazila of Basra affirmed will (al-irāda), except that they made it an attribute that is originated (ḥāditha) subsisting by itself, not in a locus. And they did not pay attention that the originatedness of will indicates renewal (tajaddud), and accordingly, the renewal of the attribute of willing (al-murīdiyya) is necessary for them because they affirm states (aḥwāl), and the renewal of the attribute of meaning (al-ma‘nawiyya) definitively indicates originatedness. So they are compelled to affirm originated states for the Pre-Eternal, exalted. For this reason, al-Ka‘bī and al-Najjār from among the Mu‘tazila went to deny this attribute as well, and made it like the attributes of negation (ṣifāt al-sulūb), meaning: that He, exalted, is not coerced nor overcome.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage concludes the classification by explaining why the author focused on the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-ma‘ānī) in the preceding discussions. The purpose is to affirm their existence and necessity, in refutation of the Mu‘tazila who deny most of them. The Mu‘tazila only affirm speech as an attribute, but they reinterpret it as an attribute of action: God creates speech (letters and sounds) in a locus, not as an eternal attribute. The author promises a later refutation. Additionally, the Mu‘tazila of Basra affirmed will (irāda) but considered it an originated accident subsisting by itself, not in a subject. The author faults them for not realizing that an originated will implies renewal (tajaddud), which would entail that the attribute of willing (murīdiyya) also changes. Since they affirm states (aḥwāl), the renewal of the state of being willing would imply a change in the deity, which is impossible. Some Mu‘tazila like al-Ka‘bī and al-Najjār therefore completely denied will, reducing it to a negative attribute: that God is not coerced or overcome. This argument highlights the Ash‘ari insistence on eternal, subsisting attributes of meaning.

Key Terms
المعاني
المعتزلة
الكلام
صفة فعلية
الإرادة
الحوادث
الأحوال
الكعبي
النجار
صفات السلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and Ash'ari agree on the nature of divine attributes.
Correction: The passage shows fundamental disagreement: Mu'tazila deny eternal attributes of meaning, reducing speech to a created action and will to an originated accident or negation, while Ash'ari affirm them as eternal and subsisting.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Mu'tazilite view of God's speech?
A) It is an eternal attribute of essence.
B) It is a created attribute consisting of letters and sounds.
C) It is identical to His knowledge.
D) It is a negative attribute.

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarī insistence on eternal attributes of meaning is defended by showing that Muʿtazilite alternatives lead to contradictions or undesirable consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been tempted to explain away one of God's attributes because it seemed too abstract or difficult to reconcile with your reason? This lesson shows that doing so leads to contradictions. Which attribute are you most tempted to reinterpret, and what does that say about your trust in revelation?
?
Sanusi argues that originated attributes imply change in God. Do you live as if God is truly unchanging in His mercy, justice, and sovereignty? Or do you sometimes act as if God's attributes are subject to your circumstances?
?
The Mu'tazila reduced God's will to a mere negation ('not coerced'). In your daily life, do you ever reduce God's will to just the absence of evil, rather than actively submitting to His eternal decree? How might you realign your attitude toward God's will as a positive, eternal attribute?

Comprehensive Attributes as a Sixth Division
Sanusi notes a sixth division of attributes: comprehensive attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of comprehensive attributes (al-sifat al-jami'a) and how they form a sixth division that encompasses all other divine attributes.

Core Idea
Comprehensive attributes gather all other divisions into a single category.

In the previous lessons, we learned about the standard five divisions of divine attributes. Now, Sanusi mentions that some scholars added a sixth division: the comprehensive attributes. These attributes are unique because they unite all other categories. Let's examine this brief but significant addition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once. Notice the key terms: الصفات الجوامع, الجامعة, الأُلوهية, الكبرياء, العظمة.
The passage is short but packs a doctrinal extension.
2
Understand the translation
Read the literal English translation. Note that 'comprehensive attributes' are said to encompass all other divisions.
3
Identify the extended classification
The passage adds a sixth division to the usual five. Understand that this is not universally accepted but is noted by some scholars.
The five standard divisions are: nafsiyya, salbiyya, ma'ani, ma'nawiyya, and fi'liyya.
4
Reflect on the examples
Consider the examples given: divinity (al-uluhiyya), majesty (al-kibriya'), and greatness (al-'azama). How do these attributes encompass all others?
These terms are broad and imply all perfections.
5
Connect to the larger framework
Think about why some scholars felt the need to add this category. It emphasizes that certain attributes summarize and include all others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفات الجوامع. وزاد بعضُهم قسماً سادساً : وهو الصفاتُ الجامعة لسائر أقسام الصفاتِ ؛ كالأُلوهية والكبرياء والعظمة.
Comprehensive attributes. Some added a sixth division: the attributes that encompass all other divisions of attributes, such as divinity, majesty, and greatness.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a classification note. It mentions that some scholars added a sixth division of divine attributes: the comprehensive attributes (al-sifat al-jami'a) that encompass all other divisions. Examples given are al-uluhiyya (divinity), al-kibriya' (majesty), and al-'azama (greatness). This is a brief addition to the standard fivefold division of attributes (nafsiyya, salbiyya, ma'ani, ma'nawiyya, and fi'liyya). The passage simply records this extended classification without further elaboration.

Sanusi's Focus on Attributes of Ma'ani and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Sanusi explains his focus on attributes of ma'ani and refutes Mu'tazilite views on speech and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi prioritizes the attributes of ma'ani and how the Mu'tazila reinterpreted speech and will.

Core Idea
Sanusi defends eternal attributes against Mu'tazilite reductions of speech and will.

Sanusi explains his pedagogical choice to focus on the attributes of meanings (al-ma'ani) because they are contested by the Mu'tazila. In this passage, he clarifies the Mu'tazilite positions on speech and will, setting the stage for later refutations. Understanding these views is crucial for grasping the Ash'ari defense of eternal attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand Sanusi's justification and the Mu'tazilite views on speech and will.
Notice that Sanusi opens by stating his focus on one division: attributes of meanings.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite positions
Extract how the Mu'tazila reinterpret speech as an attribute of action and will as a contingent attribute.
The passage mentions two groups: general Mu'tazila and Basran Mu'tazila.
3
Connect to the broader context
Recognize that this passage frames the debate over the attributes of ma'ani, promising a refutation later.
Sanusi's focus is polemical: to affirm what the Mu'tazila deny or reinterpret.

We only dealt in these preliminary points with one division: the attributes of meanings (al-ma'ani); taking care to affirm them, and indicating the necessity of their existence, in refutation of the Mu'tazila who denied them and only affirmed from among them speech, and made it an attribute of action, based on their restricting speech to letters and sounds. So the meaning of His being speaking (mutakallim) according to them is that He is an agent of speech, creating it in a locus. And there will come, God willing, the refutation of them when we come to explaining the eternal speech. And also the Mu'tazila of Basra affirmed will, except that they made it a contingent attribute subsisting in itself, not in a locus.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage explains the author's pedagogical choice. He focuses on the attributes of ma'ani (meanings) because they are denied by the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila only accept speech among the attributes of ma'ani, but they reinterpret speech as an attribute of action (fi'liyya) since they confine speech to letters and sounds. For them, God being speaking means He creates speech in a locus (e.g., the angel or the tree). The author promises a future refutation. Then he notes that the Basran Mu'tazila also affirmed will (al-irada) but made it a contingent (haditha) attribute subsisting in itself (qā'ima bi-nafsihā), not in a locus. This is a distinct position. The passage sets up the polemical context for the discussion of divine attributes.

Mu'tazilite Doubts on Attributes of Ma'ani
Sanusi recounts Mu'tazilite arguments and begins refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite arguments against the Ash'ari doctrine of attributes of ma'ani and the key refutation that the relation between attributes and judgments is one of concomitance, not causation.

Core Idea
Attributes are eternal concomitants, not temporal causes.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the core Mu'tazilite objections to the Ash'ari notion of attributes of ma'ani—real, eternal attributes subsisting in God. After summarizing their arguments, he begins to refute the first objection by clarifying that the relationship between an attribute and its resultant judgment is one of necessary concomitance, not efficient causation. This distinction is crucial for defending the orthodox position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite arguments
Carefully read the passage where Sanusi lists the three arguments: contingency, multiplicity, and polytheism.
Notice how each argument builds on the assumption that attributes would be like created entities.
2
Identify the first refutation
Notice how Sanusi responds to the first argument by redefining the meaning of ta'lil as concomitance (mulazama).
He does not yet address the multiplicity argument; this refutation is partial.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this refutation defends the eternity and necessity of attributes without implying temporality.
The key is that concomitance does not entail causation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبه المعتزلة في نفيهم لصفات المعاني والرد عليهم. الحاصل : أنَّ المعتزلة كلَّهم أنكروا صفاتِ المعاني التي أثبتها جماعةُ أهل السنَّةِ ، ووافقوهم على اتِّصافِهِ تعالى بأحكامها المعنوية ; وهي كونه تعالى قادراً، ومريداً وعالماً، وحيّاً ، وسميعاً ، وبصيراً، ومُتكلّماً ، وقالوا : يجب أن تكونَ هذهِ الأحكام واجبةً لذاته تعالى ، ولا نُعلّلها بصفاتِ المعاني كما في الشاهد ; لما يلزمُ على تعليلها في حقِّهِ تعالى : مِنْ جوازها ، وافتقارها إلى عللها ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثها ، واتصافه تعالى بالحوادثِ مستحيل ، وأيضاً : يلزم على إثباتها كثرة القدماء ، والإجماع على أنَّ القديم واحدٌ ، بل ويلزم على إثباتها : تعدُّد الإله ; لأنَّها تكون حينئذٍ مشاركة للإله في القدم ، والقدمُ أخص صفات الإله ، والمشاركة في الأخص تُوجِبُ المشاركة في الأعم، فيلزمُ أنْ تشاركَهُ تعالى في سائر صفاتِ الأُلوهِيَّةِ. وهذا الذي تخيلوه فاسد : أما ما اغترُّوا بِهِ مِنْ إطلاقِ تعليلِ الأحكام المعنوية بالمعاني : فلا يلزم منه جوازها ولا حدوثها ; لأنَّ معنى تعليلها بها : أنَّها ملازمة لها ، لا يمكنُ ثبوتها بدونها ، وكلُّ منهما قديم واجب ، وليس معناه أنَّ صفاتِ المعاني أثَرَتْ في ثبوتِ الصفاتِ المعنوية وأفادَتْها الثبوت والحصول ، وإذا كان التعليلُ بمعنى التلازم فلا يدلُّ على جواز
The doubts of the Mu'tazila in their denial of the attributes of meanings and the refutation thereof. The summary: The Mu'tazila all denied the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) which the community of Ahl al-Sunna affirmed, yet they agreed with them that He is described with their resultant judgments (al-ahkam al-ma'nawiyya); namely, that He is powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, speaking. They said: These judgments must be necessary for His essence, and we do not attribute them to attributes of meanings as in the witnessed world; because attributing them in His case entails their contingency (jawaz), their need for causes, and that entails their temporality (huduth), and His being described with temporal things is impossible. Also, affirming them entails multiplicity of eternal beings (kathrat al-qudama'), while there is consensus that the Eternal is one. Indeed, affirming them entails multiplicity of gods (ta'addud al-ilah); because they would then be sharing with God in eternity (qidam), and eternity is the most specific attribute of divinity, and sharing in the most specific necessitates sharing in the general, so it would follow that they share with Him in all attributes of divinity. And this which they imagined is corrupt: As for what they were deceived by from the absolute application of attributing the resultant judgments (ahkam ma'nawiyya) to the attributes of meanings (ma'ani): it does not entail their contingency nor their temporality; because the meaning of attributing them to them is that they are concomitant to them, their establishment without them is not possible, and each of them is eternal, necessary, and it is not meant that the attributes of meanings produced the establishment of the resultant judgments and gave them existence and obtainment. And if the attribution is in the sense of concomitance, it does not indicate contingency...
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite arguments (shubah) against the Ash'ari doctrine of attributes of ma'ani, followed by the beginning of the refutation. The Mu'tazila accept that God is described with the resultant judgments (being powerful, knowing, etc.) but deny that these judgments are caused by underlying attributes (power, knowledge, etc.) subsisting in God. Their arguments: (1) Attributing judgments to attributes would entail that the judgments are contingent and need causes, which would make them temporal (haditha), and God cannot be characterized by temporal accidents. (2) Affirming multiple eternal attributes would imply multiple eternal beings, contradicting the unity of the Eternal (qadim). (3) It would even imply multiple gods because those attributes would share in eternity, which is the most specific attribute of divinity, leading to sharing in all divine attributes. The author declares this corrupt. He begins refuting the first argument: the term 'ta'lil' (attribution) does not mean that the attributes cause the judgments as efficient causes. Rather it means mutual concomitance (mulazama): the judgment cannot exist without the attribute, and both are eternal and necessary. The relation is not one of production. So it does not entail contingency or temporality. The text is cut off at 'it does not indicate contingency...' but the argument is that since it is mere concomitance, the problem of temporality does not arise. This is a complete thought for the first refutation. The other arguments (multiplicity) are not yet refuted in the available text.

Correction: Sanusi explains that the relationship is one of necessary concomitance (mulazama), not efficient causation. Both the attribute and the judgment are eternal and necessary.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite objections to attributes of ma'ani are countered by understanding the relation as eternal concomitance, not temporal causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever found yourself resisting a truth about God because it seemed to conflict with your own logic? How does the Ash'ari insistence on concomitance rather than causation challenge your default way of reasoning about the divine? Sanusi says the attributes do not 'produce' the judgments; they merely accompany them. The Mu'tazilite Objection Against Attributes of Meanings
The Mu'tazila present two arguments against attributes of meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main Mu'tazilite arguments against affirming real attributes of meanings in God.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argued that attributes of meanings imply temporality or polytheism.

The Mu'tazila denied that God has real attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) underlying His essential descriptions. They agreed that God is described as powerful, knowing, etc., but argued that these judgments must be necessary for His essence, not caused by attributes. In this passage, we encounter their two key objections: first, that affirming real attributes would make those judgments contingent and originated, which is impossible for God; second, that it would lead to multiple eternal entities or even multiple gods.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the debate
Recall that Ahl al-Sunna affirm attributes of meanings (like power, knowledge, will) as real eternal attributes subsisting in God. The Mu'tazila reject them. Read the opening lines to see the point of agreement and disagreement.
The Mu'tazila agree on the meaningful judgments (aḥkām maʿnawiyya) but deny that these judgments are caused by separate attributes.
2
Identify the first argument: contingency and origination
Find where the Mu'tazila say that if the judgments were caused by attributes, they would be contingent and need causes, leading to temporality (ḥudūth) which is impossible for God.
The argument: any caused thing is contingent and originated; God cannot be subject to originated things.
3
Identify the second argument: multiplicity of the eternal
Look for the claim that affirming multiple eternal attributes implies multiple eternals, violating consensus that God is one, and even leads to polytheism because they share in the most specific attribute (eternity).
The Mu'tazila argue that sharing in qidam (eternity) means sharing in all divine attributes.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends with the author stating that this imagined reasoning is corrupt. In the next lesson we see the refutation. For now, note the structure of the objection.

شبه المعتزلة في نفيهم لصفات المعاني والرد عليهم ] والحاصل : أن المعتزلة كلهم أنكروا صفات المعاني التي أثبتها جماعة أهل السنة، ووافقوهم على اتصافه تعالى بأحكامها المعنوية؛ وهي كونه تعالى قادراً ومريداً وعالماً وحيّاً وسميعاً وبصيراً ومتكلّماً، وقالوا: يجب أن تكون هذه الأحكام واجبة لذاته تعالى، ولا نعللها بصفات المعاني كما في الشاهد (1); لما يلزم على تعليلها في حقه تعالى: من جوازها، وافتقارها إلى عللها، وذلك يستلزم حدوثها، واتصافه تعالى بالحوادث مستحيل، وأيضاً: يلزم على إثباتها كثرة القدماء، والإجماع على أن القديم واحد، بل ويلزم على إثباتها: تعدُّد الإله؛ لأنها تكون حينئذٍ مشاركة للإله في القدم، والقدم أخص صفات الإله، والمشاركة في الأخص توجِب المشاركة في الأعم، فيلزم أن تشاركه تعالى في سائر صفات الألوهية. وهذا الذي تخيلوه فاسد: أما ما اغتروا به من إطلاق تعليل الأحكام المعنوية بالمعاني: فلا يلزم منه جوازها ولا حدوثها؛ لأن معنى تعليلها بها: أنها ملازمة لها، لا يمكن ثبوتها بدونها، وكل منهما قديم واجب، وليس معناه أن صفات المعاني أثرت في ثبوت الصفات المعنوية وأفادتها الثبوت والحصول، وإذا كان التعليل بمعنى التلازم فلا يدل على جواز ولا على حدوث؛ إذ كما يتلازم جائزان في الشاهد يتلازم واجبان في الغائب، ولا يقدح ذلك في وجوبها، وذلك كما تقول: كونه تعالى قادراً ملازم لكونه تعالى مريداً، وهما متلازمان لكونه تعالى عالماً وإنما أطلقوا على صفات المعاني العلل دون المعنوية (1): لأن صفات المعاني صفات وجودية تتميز وتعقل على حيالها، والصفات المعنوية صفات ثبوتية لا تعقل على حيالها، وإنما تعقل بصفات المعاني، فلما كانت تابعة لها في التعقل.. أطلقوا على ما كان أصلاً في التعقل: علة، وعلى ما كان تابعاً له في التعقل: معلولاً (2) (2) ولا علة ولا معلول على الحقيقة ، وإنما هو تلازم شرطي عقلي؛ إذ لا يعقل أن يقال: فلان طويل بلا طول، وأبيضُ بلا بياض، وكذا لا يقال: عليم بلا علم، ونحو هذا، وانظر ) شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ( ص ٣٢٠ )
The doubts of the Mu'tazila in their denial of the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) and the refutation of them. The summary: that the Mu'tazila all denied the attributes of meanings which the group of Ahl al-Sunna affirmed, and agreed with them on His being described, exalted be He, with their meaningful judgments (aḥkām maʿnawiyya); they are His being, exalted be He, powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, and speaking. They said: It is necessary that these judgments be necessary for His essence, exalted be He, and we do not attribute them to attributes of meanings as in the witnessed world (al-shāhid); because of what follows from attributing them in His right, exalted be He: their contingency (jawāz) and their need for their causes, and that entails their origination (ḥudūth), and His being described, exalted be He, with originated things (ḥawādith) is impossible. Also: from affirming them follows multiplicity of the eternal (kathrat al-qudamā'), and consensus that the Eternal (al-qadīm) is one. Rather, from affirming them follows multiplicity of gods (taʿaddud al-ilāh); because they would then be sharing with God in eternity (qidam), and eternity is the most specific attribute of God, and sharing in the most specific necessitates sharing in the more general, so it would necessitate that they share with Him, exalted be He, in the rest of the attributes of divinity (ulūhiyya). And this which they imagined is corrupt. As for that with which they were deluded from the application of attributing the meaningful judgments to the meanings: it does not necessitate their contingency nor their origination; because the meaning of attributing them to them is that they are concomitant (mulāzama) with them, it is not possible for them to be established without them, and each of them is eternal and necessary. And it is not its meaning that the attributes of meanings effected the establishment of the meaningful attributes and conferred upon them establishment and occurrence. And if the attribution is in the sense of concomitance, then it does not indicate contingency nor origination; just as two contingent things are concomitant in the witnessed world, so two necessary things are concomitant in the unseen (al-ghā'ib), and that does not harm their necessity. That is as you say: His being, exalted be He, powerful is concomitant with His being, exalted be He, willing, and they are both concomitant with His being, exalted be He, knowing. And they only applied (the term) 'causes' (ʿilal) to attributes of meanings, not to the meaningful ones, because attributes of meanings are existential attributes (ṣifāt wujūdiyya) which are distinguished and intelligible by themselves, while the meaningful attributes are attributes of affirmation (ṣifāt thubūtiyya) not intelligible by themselves, but are only intelligible through the attributes of meanings. So when they were consequent upon them in intellection, they applied to that which was the origin in intellection: 'cause' (ʿilla), and to that which was consequent upon it in intellection: 'effect' (maʿlūl). (Footnote: It is not a cause nor an effect in reality; it is only a conditional rational concomitance; for it is not intelligible to say: Zayd is tall without tallness, white without whiteness, and likewise it is not said: knowledgeable without knowledge, and the like. And see Sharḥ al-ʿAqīda al-Kubrā, p. 320.)
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazilite argument against the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) and the author's refutation. The author begins by summarizing the Mu'tazilite position: they deny the existence of real attributes (meanings) underlying God's essential descriptions (such as power, will, knowledge, etc.), while affirming that God is indeed described by these judgments (aḥkām maʿnawiyya) as necessary for His essence. Their argument: if these judgments were caused by attributes of meanings, they would be contingent (jā'iz) and in need of their causes, implying temporality (ḥudūth), and God cannot be subject to temporal events. Moreover, affirming multiple eternal attributes would contradict the consensus (ijmāʿ) that the Eternal is one, and even lead to polytheism since they would share in the most specific divine attribute (qidam). The author then declares this reasoning corrupt. First, he refutes the notion of 'causation' (taʿlīl): attributing the meaningful judgments to attributes of meanings does not imply temporal origination or contingency, because the relationship is not efficient causation but necessary concomitance (mulāzama). Each is eternal and necessary; they coexist without one producing the other. The author illustrates: God's being powerful is concomitant with His being willing, and both with His being knowing. He then explains the terminology: attributes of meanings are called 'causes' (ʿilal) only because they are independently intelligible, while the meaningful attributes depend on them for conceptualization; this is a logical relationship, not causal. A footnote reinforces that it is merely a conditional rational concomitance, not real causality.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Mu'tazilite arguments are sound and that Ahl al-Sunna affirm multiple eternal entities.
Correction: The Mu'tazila incorrectly assume that the relationship between attributes and judgments is causal (causing contingency/temporality) and that multiple attributes imply multiple gods; the Sunna response shows the relationship is necessary concomitance.

A) Because attributes are created in time
B) Because the judgments would be contingent and need causes
C) Because God cannot have any attributes
D) Because the attributes would be multiple

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argued that affirming real attributes makes God's descriptions contingent and implies multiple eternal beings, but their reasoning confuses causal necessity with logical concomitance.

The Refutation: Concomitance Not Causation
The author refutes the Mu'tazilite argument by redefining 'attribution' as concomitance and clarifying terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the Mu'tazilite objection by distinguishing causation from necessary concomitance and explaining the terminological convention.

Core Idea
Attributing meaningful judgments to attributes of meanings means necessary coexistence, not efficient causation.

In this passage, the author directly refutes the Mu'tazilite arguments. He clarifies that 'attributing' (taʿlīl) the meaningful judgments to the attributes of meanings does not imply that the attributes produce the judgments or cause them to occur. Instead, the relationship is one of necessary concomitance (mulāzama): the judgment cannot exist without the attribute, and both are eternal and necessary. He also explains why the scholars called the attributes 'causes' (ʿilal) — it is a logical priority in understanding, not real causality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core move: refutation of causality
Read the opening of the refutation: 'As for that with which they were deluded... it does not necessitate their contingency nor their origination.' The author denies that the relationship is causal. Instead, it is concomitance.
The key term: 'mulāzama' (concomitance).
2
Grasp the definition of 'taʿlīl' as concomitance
The author states: 'the meaning of attributing them to them is that they are concomitant with them, it is not possible for them to be established without them'. This means the judgment always accompanies the attribute, not that the attribute produces it.
Both the attribute and the judgment are eternal and necessary.
3
Apply the distinction to the Mu'tazilite charge
Since it is only concomitance, the judgments are not contingent or originated; they are necessary like the attributes. The example: God's being powerful is concomitant with His being willing, both with His being knowing.
Concomitance occurs among necessary things as well as among contingent ones.
4
Understand the terminological clarification
The author explains why attributes of meanings are called 'causes' and the meaningful judgments 'effects': because the attributes are intelligible by themselves, while the judgments are understood only through the attributes. This is a conceptual dependence, not real causation.
A footnote adds that it is merely a conditional rational concomitance, not real causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبه المعتزلة في نفيهم لصفات المعاني والرد عليهم ] والحاصل : أن المعتزلة كلهم أنكروا صفات المعاني التي أثبتها جماعة أهل السنة، ووافقوهم على اتصافه تعالى بأحكامها المعنوية؛ وهي كونه تعالى قادراً ومريداً وعالماً وحيّاً وسميعاً وبصيراً ومتكلّماً، وقالوا: يجب أن تكون هذه الأحكام واجبة لذاته تعالى، ولا نعللها بصفات المعاني كما في الشاهد (1); لما يلزم على تعليلها في حقه تعالى: من جوازها، وافتقارها إلى عللها، وذلك يستلزم حدوثها، واتصافه تعالى بالحوادث مستحيل، وأيضاً: يلزم على إثباتها كثرة القدماء، والإجماع على أن القديم واحد، بل ويلزم على إثباتها: تعدُّد الإله؛ لأنها تكون حينئذٍ مشاركة للإله في القدم، والقدم أخص صفات الإله، والمشاركة في الأخص توجِب المشاركة في الأعم، فيلزم أن تشاركه تعالى في سائر صفات الألوهية. وهذا الذي تخيلوه فاسد: أما ما اغتروا به من إطلاق تعليل الأحكام المعنوية بالمعاني: فلا يلزم منه جوازها ولا حدوثها؛ لأن معنى تعليلها بها: أنها ملازمة لها، لا يمكن ثبوتها بدونها، وكل منهما قديم واجب، وليس معناه أن صفات المعاني أثرت في ثبوت الصفات المعنوية وأفادتها الثبوت والحصول، وإذا كان التعليل بمعنى التلازم فلا يدل على جواز ولا على حدوث؛ إذ كما يتلازم جائزان في الشاهد يتلازم واجبان في الغائب، ولا يقدح ذلك في وجوبها، وذلك كما تقول: كونه تعالى قادراً ملازم لكونه تعالى مريداً، وهما متلازمان لكونه تعالى عالماً وإنما أطلقوا على صفات المعاني العلل دون المعنوية (1): لأن صفات المعاني صفات وجودية تتميز وتعقل على حيالها، والصفات المعنوية صفات ثبوتية لا تعقل على حيالها، وإنما تعقل بصفات المعاني، فلما كانت تابعة لها في التعقل.. أطلقوا على ما كان أصلاً في التعقل: علة، وعلى ما كان تابعاً له في التعقل: معلولاً (2) (2) ولا علة ولا معلول على الحقيقة ، وإنما هو تلازم شرطي عقلي؛ إذ لا يعقل أن يقال: فلان طويل بلا طول، وأبيضُ بلا بياض، وكذا لا يقال: عليم بلا علم، ونحو هذا، وانظر ) شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ( ص ٣٢٠ )
The doubts of the Mu'tazila in their denial of the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) and the refutation of them. The summary: that the Mu'tazila all denied the attributes of meanings which the group of Ahl al-Sunna affirmed, and agreed with them on His being described, exalted be He, with their meaningful judgments (aḥkām maʿnawiyya); they are His being, exalted be He, powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, and speaking. They said: It is necessary that these judgments be necessary for His essence, exalted be He, and we do not attribute them to attributes of meanings as in the witnessed world (al-shāhid); because of what follows from attributing them in His right, exalted be He: their contingency (jawāz) and their need for their causes, and that entails their origination (ḥudūth), and His being described, exalted be He, with originated things (ḥawādith) is impossible. Also: from affirming them follows multiplicity of the eternal (kathrat al-qudamā'), and consensus that the Eternal (al-qadīm) is one. Rather, from affirming them follows multiplicity of gods (taʿaddud al-ilāh); because they would then be sharing with God in eternity (qidam), and eternity is the most specific attribute of God, and sharing in the most specific necessitates sharing in the more general, so it would necessitate that they share with Him, exalted be He, in the rest of the attributes of divinity (ulūhiyya). And this which they imagined is corrupt. As for that with which they were deluded from the application of attributing the meaningful judgments to the meanings: it does not necessitate their contingency nor their origination; because the meaning of attributing them to them is that they are concomitant (mulāzama) with them, it is not possible for them to be established without them, and each of them is eternal and necessary. And it is not its meaning that the attributes of meanings effected the establishment of the meaningful attributes and conferred upon them establishment and occurrence. And if the attribution is in the sense of concomitance, then it does not indicate contingency nor origination; just as two contingent things are concomitant in the witnessed world, so two necessary things are concomitant in the unseen (al-ghā'ib), and that does not harm their necessity. That is as you say: His being, exalted be He, powerful is concomitant with His being, exalted be He, willing, and they are both concomitant with His being, exalted be He, knowing. And they only applied (the term) 'causes' (ʿilal) to attributes of meanings, not to the meaningful ones, because attributes of meanings are existential attributes (ṣifāt wujūdiyya) which are distinguished and intelligible by themselves, while the meaningful attributes are attributes of affirmation (ṣifāt thubūtiyya) not intelligible by themselves, but are only intelligible through the attributes of meanings. So when they were consequent upon them in intellection, they applied to that which was the origin in intellection: 'cause' (ʿilla), and to that which was consequent upon it in intellection: 'effect' (maʿlūl). (Footnote: It is not a cause nor an effect in reality; it is only a conditional rational concomitance; for it is not intelligible to say: Zayd is tall without tallness, white without whiteness, and likewise it is not said: knowledgeable without knowledge, and the like. And see Sharḥ al-ʿAqīda al-Kubrā, p. 320.)
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazilite argument against the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) and the author's refutation. The author begins by summarizing the Mu'tazilite position: they deny the existence of real attributes (meanings) underlying God's essential descriptions (such as power, will, knowledge, etc.), while affirming that God is indeed described by these judgments (aḥkām maʿnawiyya) as necessary for His essence. Their argument: if these judgments were caused by attributes of meanings, they would be contingent (jā'iz) and in need of their causes, implying temporality (ḥudūth), and God cannot be subject to temporal events. Moreover, affirming multiple eternal attributes would contradict the consensus (ijmāʿ) that the Eternal is one, and even lead to polytheism since they would share in the most specific divine attribute (qidam). The author then declares this reasoning corrupt. First, he refutes the notion of 'causation' (taʿlīl): attributing the meaningful judgments to attributes of meanings does not imply temporal origination or contingency, because the relationship is not efficient causation but necessary concomitance (mulāzama). Each is eternal and necessary; they coexist without one producing the other. The author illustrates: God's being powerful is concomitant with His being willing, and both with His being knowing. He then explains the terminology: attributes of meanings are called 'causes' (ʿilal) only because they are independently intelligible, while the meaningful attributes depend on them for conceptualization; this is a logical relationship, not causal. A footnote reinforces that it is merely a conditional rational concomitance, not real causality.

تلازم

حدوث
علة
معلول
صفات المعاني
الصفات المعنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the term 'cause' (ʿilla) in the context of attributes implies that attributes temporally produce the judgments.
Correction: The author explicitly states it is not real causation but logical concomitance; the terminology is due to conceptual priority.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does attributing the meaningful judgments to the attributes of meanings not imply temporality?
A) Because the attributes are eternal and the judgments are contingent
B) Because the attribution is simply a statement of necessary concomitance, not causation
C) Because the judgments are not real
D) Because the attributes are not eternal

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that attributing judgments to attributes does not entail causation; it is a relationship of necessary concomitance between eternal realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is powerful,' do you ever unconsciously imagine His power as something separate that 'causes' His actions? How does this lesson's insistence on coincident instead of causation challenge your mental picture of God? Will you now actively remind yourself that His attributes are not causes but necessary truths about Him?
?
Have you ever slipped into thinking of God's attributes as if they were created qualities that 'come into effect' when He chooses to act? This lesson says they are eternal and their connection to His actions is mere concomitant. What would change in your worship if you fully internalized that there is no temporal sequence in God—no before and after—only eternal, necessary reality?
?
The author uses the term 'cause' for attributes only as a manner of speaking. Can you identify a habit in your own religious language where a term you use about God might be misleading? How will you ensure your words align with what you truly believe about His transcendence? Make a specific resolution to correct one such phrase in your daily prayers or discussions.

Refutation of the Multiplicity of Eternal Beings Objection
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that affirming multiple attributes entails multiple eternal beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming multiple divine attributes does not entail multiple eternal beings, and how the Ash'ari response uses the distinction between essence and attributes to refute the Mu'tazilite appeal to consensus.

Core Idea
Attributes do not multiply the essence; the One remains one.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if God has multiple eternal attributes, then there would be multiple eternal beings, violating Islamic consensus. Sanusi refutes this by clarifying that multiplicity applies to essences, not to attributes. In this lesson, we will examine his precise argument and see how it preserves tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: objection, then refutation.
The passage is a single paragraph with a footnote integrated.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection
The Mu'tazilites claimed that affirming multiple eternal attributes contradicts the consensus that 'the Eternal is one', implying multiple eternal beings (qudamā'). Identify this as the starting point.
3
Identify Sanusi's key distinction
Sanusi argues that multiplicity applies to the thing itself, not to its attributes. The essence is one regardless of how many attributes it has. Note the phrase: 'because a thing does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its attributes.'
This is the core logical move.
4
Clarify the object of consensus
Sanusi says the consensus is on the unity of the essence described with divine attributes, not on the unity of an unrestricted 'thing described by eternity'. Understand why this distinction matters.
The footnote explains that multiplicity can only be in essences or parts, not attributes.
5
See the conclusion as sophistry dismissal
Sanusi concludes that the Mu'tazilite appeal to consensus is obfuscation and sophistry (talbīs wa mughālata). Reflect on how this refutes the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما ألزموه من مخالفة الإجماع بتكثير القدماء: ففاسد؛ لأن الشيء لا يتكثر بكثرة صفاته (3)، فالذات القديمة واحدة بإجماع وإن تعدَّدَتْ صفاتها، فمتعلَّقُ الإجماع وحدة الذات الموصوفة بصفات الألوهية، لا وحدة الموصوف بالقدم من غير تقييد بكونه ذاتاً (3) إذ الكثرة تتصوّر بتعدُّد الذات، أو تعدُّد أجزاء الذات الواحدة، وكلا المعنيين منفي في مسألة إثبات صفات المعاني، فالاحتجاج بالإجماع من قبلهم تلبيس ومغالطة
As for what they required [against us] of violating consensus by multiplying the eternal beings: it is corrupt; because a thing does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its attributes. The eternal essence is one by consensus, even if its attributes are multiple. So the object of consensus is the unity of the essence described with the attributes of divinity, not the unity of that which is described by eternity without restriction to being an essence. (Footnote: For multiplicity is conceived by multiplicity of the essence, or multiplicity of parts of one essence, and both meanings are negated in the issue of affirming attributes of meanings, so their argument from consensus is obfuscation and sophistry.)
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage refutes the second Mu'tazilite objection: that affirming multiple eternal attributes would contradict the consensus (ijmāʿ) that 'the Eternal is one', implying multiple eternal beings (qudamā'). The author argues that multiplicity applies to the thing itself, not to its attributes. The essence is one, whether it has one attribute or many. The consensus is on the unity of the essence (dhāt) described with divine attributes, not on the unity of an indeterminate 'thing described by eternity' that might be an attribute itself. The footnote clarifies that multiplicity can only be imagined as multiple essences or multiple parts of one essence; neither applies to the attributes, which are neither separate essences nor parts of the essence. Therefore, the Mu'tazilite appeal to consensus is fallacious (talbīs wa mughālata).

تلبيس

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple eternal attributes would mean multiple gods.
Correction: Attributes are not independent entities; they are descriptions of the one essence. Multiplicity applies to essences, not to attributes.

Checkpoint

A) Because attributes are essentially multiple by nature
B) Because consensus only applies to the essence, not to attributes
C) Because consensus does not count in such matters
D) Because the attributes are temporal

Key Takeaway
The unity of God's essence is not compromised by the multiplicity of His attributes, because attributes are not separate beings.

Refuting the Polytheism Accusation: Eternity and Divinity
Sanusi refutes Mu'tazilite polytheism accusation using the bi-dhātihī/bi-ghayrihī distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sharing in the attribute of eternity does not imply sharing in divinity, and how Sanusi uses the distinction between qadīm bi-dhātihī and qadīm bi-ghayrihī to defend divine uniqueness.

Core Idea
Sharing eternity does not make something divine; only the essence is divine by itself.

Sanusi now tackles a third Mu'tazilite objection: if God's attributes share in His most specific attribute—eternity—then they would be gods themselves, leading to polytheism. This is a serious charge, but Sanusi shows it rests on a misunderstanding of what eternity means. Let's follow his careful refutation, which hinges on distinguishing two ways something can be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow: objection, response, proof, analogy.
Notice that the passage begins with 'وأما ما ألزموه...' which indicates this is a refutation.
2
Identify the key distinction
Sanusi introduces a critical distinction between 'eternal by itself' (qadīm bi-dhātihī) and 'eternal by another' (qadīm bi-ghayrihī). Find this in the text and understand why it matters.
This distinction is the core of the argument.
3
Understand the analogy with life
Sanusi gives an analogy: sharing life does not make one human. Apply this to the divine attributes. Why is this analogy effective?
Think about how 'life' is common to humans, animals, and plants, yet it doesn't define humanity.
4
Reflect on the misconception
The Mu'tazilites assume that any eternal being must be divine. Sanusi corrects this. Ask yourself: if a thing is eternal by another (like an attribute), does it deserve worship?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما ألزموه من تعدُّد الألوهية بسبب اشتراكها في أخص صفات الإله؛ وهو القدم: ففاسد؛ لأن القدم ليس صفة نفسية؛ بدليل أنك لا تعقل إلا قديماً بذاته، وقديماً بغيره، فالقدم غير ذاتي لكل واحد منهما، بخلاف الألوهية فإنها وصف ذاتي لمن يقوم به، فليس القدم هو الإله، بل الإله هو الموصوف بالقدم مع صفات أخرى، فمشاركة الصفات للذات في القدم لا يوجب مشاركتها في الألوهية، كما أن مشاركة زيد لعمرو في الحياة لا توجب مشاركته في الإنسان؛ لأن الحياة غير مختصة بالإنسان، بل يعم الحيوان والنبات
As for what they required [against us] of multiplicity of divinity (taʿaddud al-ulūhiyya) due to their sharing in the most specific attribute of God (aṣaṣ ṣifāt al-ilāh), which is eternity (qidam): it is corrupt; because eternity is not a self-subsistent attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya); the proof is that you do not conceive except an eternally existent by itself (qadīm bi-dhātihī) and an eternally existent by another (qadīm bi-ghayrihī). So eternity is not essential (dhātī) for each of them, unlike divinity (ulūhiyya), for it is an essential description (waṣf dhātī) for the one who is described by it. So sharing of attributes with the essence in eternity does not necessitate their sharing in divinity, just as the sharing of Zayd with ʿAmr in life does not necessitate his sharing in being human; because life is not specific to humans, but encompasses animals and plants.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage refutes the third Mu'tazilite objection: if attributes share in the most specific attribute of God (eternity, qidam), they would become gods themselves, leading to polytheism. The author responds that eternity is not a self-subsistent attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya) that defines the essence. Rather, there are two types of eternal: eternal by itself (qadīm bi-dhātihī, i.e., God) and eternal by another (qadīm bi-ghayrihī, i.e., the attributes, which are eternal because the essence is eternal). Eternity is not essential (dhātī) to the attributes; they are eternal derivatively. In contrast, divinity (ulūhiyya) is an essential description of the essence alone. Therefore, sharing in the accidental attribute of eternity does not imply sharing in divinity. The author draws an analogy: Zayd and ʿAmr both share life, but that does not make ʿAmr human if he is not; life is a common attribute across species. Similarly, eternity is common to the essence and attributes, but divinity remains specific to the essence.

Key Terms
تعدد الألوهية
أخص صفات الإله
قدم
صفة نفسية
قديم بذاته
قديم بغيره
وصف ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anything that shares in the attribute of eternity must be divine and worthy of worship.
Correction: Eternity can be accidental (qadīm bi-ghayrihī), as in the case of attributes, so it is not a defining characteristic of divinity. Only the essence is divine by itself.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that sharing in eternity does not imply sharing in divinity?
A) Eternity is not an attribute at all
B) Eternity is a necessary attribute of the essence only
C) Eternity can be derived (bi-ghayrihī) and so is not exclusive to God
D) The Mu'tazila misunderstood the meaning of eternity

Key Takeaway
Eternity is not exclusive to divinity; it can be an accidental attribute, so sharing eternity does not imply polytheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does Sanusi's distinction between 'eternal by itself' and 'eternal by another' expose the hidden shirk in such a attachment? Will you now relegate that thing to its proper place as a creature, not the Creator?
?
The Mu'tazilites made an intellectual error by conflating a shared attribute with divinity. Are there any theological concepts you hold that might be based on a similar conflation? Ask yourself honestly: have you ever equated a quality of a righteous person (like knowledge or power) with God's exclusive right to that quality? What would it mean to repent from that subtle association?
?
Sanusi's argument requires precise reasoning. Do you have the intellectual humility to submit your own understanding of God to the rigorous distinctions laid out by the scholars, even when your heart resists? What would change in your worship if you fully embraced that only the Essence is divine by itself, and everything else—even the most exalted attributes—is dependent upon Him?

Necessity of Attributes in the Unseen
Sanusi presents the analogy of mutual necessity between witnessed and unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the analogy of mutual necessity between witnessed and unseen to show that necessary attributes can be necessarily connected without implying contingency.

Core Idea
Attributes in the unseen follow the same logical necessity as in the witnessed.

Sanusi addresses a potential objection: if attributes are necessarily connected, does that make them contingent? He shows that just as two contingent things can be mutually necessary in the witnessed world, two necessary things can also be mutually necessary in the unseen world, and this does not undermine their necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting Sanusi's claim that mutual necessity does not detract from necessity.
The passage begins with 'ولا على حدوث'.
2
Understand the analogy
Sanusi compares the relationship of attributes in the unseen to that in the witnessed: just as contingent attributes can be mutually necessary, so can necessary ones.
The key is that mutual necessity is a logical relationship, not a temporal one.
3
Examine the example
He gives the example of power, will, and knowledge being mutually necessary.
This shows that the attributes are inseparable.
4
Ask why this matters
This argument defends the coherence of affirming multiple attributes of God without compromising their necessity.
It is a foundational move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا على حدوث ؛ إذْ كما يتلازم جائزان في الشاهد يتلازم واجبانِ في الغائبِ ، ولا يقدح ذلك في وجوبها ، وذلك كما تقولُ : كونه تعالى قادراً ملازم لكونه تعالى مريداً ، وهما متلازمان لكونه تعالى عالماً
وإنَّما أطلقوا على صفاتِ المعاني العلل دون المعنوية : لأنَّ صفاتِ المعاني صفات وجوديَّةٌ تتميَّرُ وتُعقَلُ على حيالها ، والصفاتِ المعنوية صفات ثبوتيَّةٌ لا تُعقَلُ على حيالها ، وإِنَّما تُعقَلُ بصفاتِ ، فلمَّا كانَتْ تابعةً لها في التعقُل .. أطلقوا على ما كانَ أصلاً في التعقل : علة ، وعلى ما كان تابعاً له في التعقل : معلولاً يعني : أهل الحق والسنة والجماعة ولا علة ولا معلول على الحقيقة ، وإنما هو تلازم شرطي عقلي ؛ إذ لا يعقل أن يقال : فلان طويل بلا طول ، وأبيضُ بلا بياض ، وكذا لا يقال : عليم بلا علم
وأما ما ألزموهُ مِنْ مخالفة الإجماع بتكثيرِ القدماء : ففاسد ؛ لأنَّ الشيء لا يتكثر بكثرة صفاتِهِ إذ الكثرة تتصوّر بتعدُّد الذات ، أو تعدُّد أجزاء الذات الواحدة ، وكلا المعنيين منفي في مسألة إثبات صفات المعاني ، فالاحتجاج بالإجماع من قبلهم تلبيس ومغالطة
And not upon occurrence; for just as two possible things are mutually necessary in the witnessed, two necessary things are mutually necessary in the unseen, and that does not detract from their necessity. For example, you say: His being, exalted, powerful is conjoined with His being, exalted, willing, and both are conjoined with His being, exalted, knowing. And they only applied the term 'cause' to the attributes of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani) and not to the mnemic attributes (sifat al-ma'nawiyya) because the attributes of meaning are existential attributes that are distinguished and conceived in themselves, while the mnemic attributes are attributes of affirmation that are not conceived in themselves, but are only conceived through attributes. So since they are consequent to them in conception, they applied to that which is the basis in conception 'cause', and to that which is consequent to it in conception 'effect' — meaning the People of Truth and the Sunnah and the Community; and there is no real cause or effect, but rather it is a conditional rational mutual necessity; for it is not reasonable to say 'So-and-so is tall without tallness', or 'white without whiteness', and likewise one does not say 'knowing without knowledge'. And as for what they forced upon them of opposing consensus by multiplying the eternals: it is corrupt; because a thing does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its attributes. For multiplicity is conceivable by the multiplicity of the essence or the multiplicity of the parts of a single essence, and both meanings are negated in the matter of establishing the attributes of meaning, so the appeal to consensus on their part is obfuscation and sophistry.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. First, Sanusi establishes that just as two contingent (ja'iz) attributes can be necessarily connected in the witnessed world (shahid), so two necessary (wajib) attributes can be necessarily connected in the unseen world (gha'ib). This does not undermine their necessity. For example, God's being powerful is necessarily connected to His being willing, and both are necessarily connected to His being knowing. He then explains why the Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox) call the attributes of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani) like power, will, and knowledge 'causes' and the mnemic attributes (sifat al-ma'nawiyya) like being powerful, willing, and knowing 'effects'. The reason is that the attributes of meaning are existential and conceivable independently, while the mnemic attributes are attributes of affirmation (thubutiyya) that are not conceivable independently but only through the attributes of meaning. So the 'cause' is the basis in conception, and the 'effect' is the consequent. However, Sanusi clarifies that this is not real causality but a conditional rational mutual necessity (talazum sharti 'aqli). He then refutes an objection that this position violates consensus by multiplying eternal entities (takthir al-qudama'). He argues that a thing does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its attributes, because multiplicity requires either multiple essences or multiple parts of a single essence. Neither applies here, so the appeal to consensus is mere obfuscation.

Key Terms

شاهد
غائب
جائزان
واجبان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That necessary attributes cannot be necessarily connected to one another, as that would imply they are contingent or occurrent.
Correction: Sanusi shows that mutual necessity is a logical relationship that applies to both contingent and necessary things; for necessary things, it does not imply occurrence.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue about mutual necessity (talazum) in the case of necessary attributes?
A) It makes them occurrent (hadith).
B) It does not detract from their necessity.
C) It only applies to possible attributes.
D) It is a sign of temporal creation.

Key Takeaway
Mutual necessity between attributes does not negate their necessity; it is a logical correlation that applies to both contingent and necessary things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God is powerful, willing, and knowing. But have you ever thought that because these are all connected, maybe one is a result of the other? The lesson shows they are mutually necessary, not cause-effect. How does that change how you think about God's essence? What will you do the next time a doubt arises about God's attributes being eternal?
?
Just as you see mutual necessity in the physical world - like fire and heat - the unseen follows a deeper necessity. What area of your spiritual life have you treated as separate from God's attributes, and how will you reconnect it starting today?
?
When you consider that God's attributes are necessarily connected, how does that affect your certainty that His will is inseparable from His power? Does your heart truly rest in that unity, or do you sometimes separate them in your mind? Commit today to reflecting on this connection whenever you say 'Insha'Allah'.

Cause and Effect in Divine Attributes
Sanusi explains the cause-effect terminology for attributes and clarifies it is conditional rational necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the People of Truth call the attributes of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani) 'causes' and the mnemic attributes (sifat al-ma'nawiyya) 'effects', and why this is not a real causal relationship but a conditional rational necessity.

Core Idea
Terms 'cause' and 'effect' refer to conceptual priority, not real causality.

Sanusi explains the terminology used by the Ahl al-Haqq: the attributes of meaning (like power, will, knowledge) are called 'cause' and the mnemic attributes (like powerful, willing, knowing) are called 'effect'. This is because attributes of meaning are conceived independently, while mnemic attributes are only conceived through them. However, he clarifies that this is not real causality but a conditional rational mutual necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on terminology
Focus on why 'cause' applies to attributes of meaning and 'effect' to mnemic attributes.
The passage contains a definition of each type of attribute.
2
Understand the conceptual distinction
Attributes of meaning are existential and conceived independently; mnemic attributes are affirmative but conceived only through the former.
This distinction is key to the terminology.
3
Note the clarification
Sanusi emphasizes that this is not real causality, but a conditional rational necessity.
He gives examples like 'tall without tallness' is unintelligible.
4
Apply the examples
Consider the examples: one cannot say 'tall without tallness' or 'knowing without knowledge'. This shows the conditional necessity.
This reinforces that mnemic attributes depend on attributes of meaning in conception.

Refutation of Multiplicity Objection
Sanusi refutes the objection that attributes multiply eternal entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the objection that affirming multiple attributes of meaning implies multiplying eternal entities, and why this objection is based on obfuscation.

Core Idea
Attributes do not multiply the essence; multiplicity applies only to essences or parts.

Sanusi addresses an objection from opponents who claim that affirming multiple attributes of meaning (like power, will, knowledge) violates consensus by multiplying eternal entities (takthir al-qudama'). He shows that this objection is invalid because a thing does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its attributes—multiplicity requires either multiple essences or multiple parts of a single essence, neither of which applies here. Thus, the appeal to consensus is mere obfuscation.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Focus on Sanusi's argument that a thing does not become multiple by its attributes.
The passage begins with 'وأما ما ألزموه'.
2
Understand the concept of multiplicity
Sanusi defines multiplicity as requiring either multiple essences (dhawat) or multiple parts of a single essence.
This is a precise logical definition.
3
Apply to attributes
Neither condition applies to attributes of meaning: they are not separate essences nor parts of the divine essence.
Thus, multiple attributes do not make God multiple.
4
Note the accusation of obfuscation
Sanusi says the opponents' appeal to consensus is 'talbis' (obfuscation) and 'mugalata' (sophistry).
This shows the strength of his refutation.
