Definition of Divine Will and Its Necessity
Sanusi defines divine will, proves its necessity, and refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of divine will, why it is necessary for specification of possibles, and how Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila restriction of will to good only.

Core Idea
Divine will specifies which possibles occur, proving God's perfection.

Sanusi now turns to the attribute of will (al-irāda). He begins by defining it precisely and then demonstrates its logical necessity: without will, God's power could not produce specific outcomes. The passage also addresses a key theological dispute with the Mu'tazila, who deny that God's will encompasses evil. By the end, you'll see why God's will is both necessary and all-encompassing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Understand the definition
Note the definition of will: an attribute by which specification of a possible is achievable. What does 'specification' mean here?
3
Follow the necessity argument
Trace Sanusi's reasoning: why is will needed? Because possibles are equally related to power, so another attribute must specify. Identify the key premise and conclusion.
The argument is based on the equality of relation between possibles and power.
4
Examine the refutations
See how Sanusi refutes the idea that God's incapability follows, then why other attributes like knowledge cannot specify, and finally the refutation of Mu'tazila.
Each refutation builds on the definition and necessity argument.
5
Reflect on the perfection
Consider why God's acting by pure will and choice is a sign of utmost perfection, not deficiency.
Connect to the Quranic verse provided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ صفة الإرادة ] وقوله : ( والإرادة : صفةٌ يتأتى بها تخصيص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليهِ ) . ا يعني : أنَّ الممكناتِ لمَّا كانَتْ نسبتها إلى قدرتِهِ تعالى على حد السواء ؛ فلو اختصَّتْ بوجودِ بعضها دونَ بعض.. لزمَ العجز ، فإذاً لا بدَّ لتخصيص بعض الممكنات بالوقوع دون مقابله .. مِنْ صفةٍ أُخرى ، وليسَتْ إلا صفة الإرادة ؛ إذْ لا يلزم نقص في قولنا : أراد الله تعالى وجود هذا الممكن ، ولم يرد هذا الممكن الآخر ، بل أرادَ عدمَهُ ، بل ذلك دليل على غاية الكمال ؛ فإِنَّ تصرُّفَهُ جَلَّ وعلا في الممكناتِ بمحضِ الإرادة والاختيار، ولا باعث له على ممكن منها ولا إكراه ولا إجبار ، كما قال جل وعلا : ﴿ وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ ويختارُ﴾ [القصص : ٦٨] . ولو قلت : قَدَرَ الله تعالى على هذا الممكن الموجود ، ولم يقدر على مقابله .. لكان فاسداً ؛ لما فيهِ مِنْ لزومِ نقيصة العجزِ وأما سائر الصفاتِ - كالعلم ، والكلام ، والسمع ، والبصر - فلا يصح التخصيص بها (۱) ؛ لأنَّ التخصيص تأثير ، وهذه الصفات ليسَتْ مُؤثرةً في مُتعلقاتِها . وأشار بالعموم في قوله : ( الممكن ( إلى فسادِ مذهب المعتزلة الذينَ خصَّصوا تعلق الإرادة بالخير دونَ الشر ، وبالصلاح والأصلح دون مقابليهما (۱) جواب عن سؤال مقدَّر ؛ تقديره : لِمَ خصصتم القدرة بصفة الإرادة ولم تخصصوها بالعلم أو الكلام أو السمع أو البصر ؟
[Attribute of Will] And His statement: 'And will is an attribute by which the specification of the possible to some of what is permissible for it is achievable.' That is, since the possibles have a relation to His power that is equal, if some of them were specified to exist to the exclusion of others, incapability would follow. Therefore, for the specification of some possibles to occur rather than their opposite, another attribute is necessary, and it is none other than the attribute of will, since no deficiency follows from our saying: God Almighty willed the existence of this possible and did not will that other possible, but rather willed its non-existence; rather that is proof of utmost perfection, for His action, Glorified and Exalted, in the possibles is by pure will and choice, and there is no cause for Him regarding any possible, nor coercion, nor compulsion, as He said, Glorified and Exalted: 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (Qur'an 28:68). And if you said: God Almighty was able for this existing possible and was not able for its opposite, that would be corrupt because of the implication of the deficiency of incapability. As for the other attributes—like knowledge, speech, hearing, and sight—specification is not valid by them, because specification is an effect, and these attributes are not effective in their objects. And by the generality in His statement 'the possible,' He indicated the corruption of the doctrine of the Mu'tazila who restricted the attachment of will to good to the exclusion of evil, and to what is beneficial and most beneficial to the exclusion of their opposites. (1) Answer to an implicit question: its estimation: Why did you specify power with the attribute of will and did not specify it with knowledge, speech, hearing, or sight?
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the divine attribute of will (al-irāda). The author first cites the definition: will is an attribute by which specification of a possible to some of its permissible states is achievable. Then he argues for its necessity: possibles are equally related to God's power; if some come to exist without specification, incapability would be implied. Hence another attribute—will—is needed to specify which possibles occur. This does not imply deficiency; rather, it shows perfection because God acts by pure choice without external cause or compulsion. The author quotes the Qur'anic verse to support this. He also warns against saying God is capable of one possible but not its opposite, as that implies incapability. Then he addresses why specification is not done by other attributes like knowledge: because specification is an effect, and those attributes are not effective. Finally, he refutes the Mu'tazila who restrict will to good and beneficial things, noting that the definition's generality includes evil as well. The footnote answers an implicit question: why will and not other attributes for specification.

Definition of Divine Knowledge
Sanusi defines divine knowledge in one sentence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of divine knowledge as an attribute of disclosure.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is the attribute that discloses reality as it is.

In this brief but foundational passage, Sanusi defines one of God's essential attributes: knowledge. This definition is the starting point for understanding how God knows all things perfectly, without error or ignorance. Let's unpack what this means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully. Notice the concise structure: one sentence defines the entire attribute.
The Arabic uses the feminine pronoun because 'attribute' (صفة) is feminine.
2
Identify the key terms
The two key terms are العلم (knowledge) and المعلوم (the known). Understand how they relate: knowledge is the means by which the known is disclosed.
The word 'ينكشف' implies a clear uncovering, not a creation.
3
Analyze the definition
Sanusi defines knowledge as an attribute by which the known is disclosed as it is. This means God's knowledge perfectly corresponds to reality without error, forgetfulness, or ignorance.
This distinguishes knowledge from other attributes like will and power.
4
Contrast with other attributes
Compare this definition with definitions of will (which specifies) and power (which brings into existence). Knowledge merely discloses; it does not create or specify.
This contrast helps avoid common confusions.

The Absolute Divine Will and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Sanusi argues for God's absolute will and refutes Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's will encompasses both good and evil, and why the Mu'tazila restriction of will to good alone implies deficiency in God.

Core Idea
God's unrestricted will is a sign of perfection, not limitation.

In this passage, Sanusi tackles a key point of contention with the Mu'tazila: Does God's will extend to all things, even evil? He argues that God's absolute freedom in willing all contingencies—without external cause—is actually proof of perfection. Moreover, he shows that the attribute of will uniquely involves effect (takhṣīṣ), unlike knowledge or speech, which merely perceive or disclose. Read carefully to see how he builds the argument and refutes the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the verse quoted and the logical flow.
2
Identify the two key claims
Sanusi makes two main points: (1) God's willing some things and not others shows perfection, not imperfection. (2) Only the attribute of will can 'specify' (takhṣīṣ) because it is effective. Find where each claim is made.
The first claim is supported by the Quranic verse; the second is explained in relation to other attributes.
3
Understand the objection and refutation
Sanusi answers a hidden objection: 'Why specify will with power?' He clarifies that specification is about effect, which only will has. Then he refutes the Mu'tazila by showing their view would imply God's will is constrained.
This connects the entire passage: the freedom of will refutes the Mu'tazila.
4
Reflect on the misconception
The common misunderstanding is that limiting God's will to good is reverent. Sanusi turns that upside down: it actually implies deficiency. Consider why that is.
Use the checkpoint to test your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أراد الله تعالى وجود هذا الممكن ، ولم يرد هذا الممكن الآخر ، بل أرادَ عدمَهُ ، بل ذلك دليل على غاية الكمال ؛ فإِنَّ تصرُّفَهُ جَلَّ وعلا في الممكناتِ بمحضِ الإرادة والاختيار، ولا باعث له على ممكن منها ولا إكراه ولا إجبار ، كما قال جل وعلا : ﴿ وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ ويختارُ﴾ [القصص : ٦٨] . ولو قلت : قَدَرَ الله تعالى على هذا الممكن الموجود ، ولم يقدر على مقابله .. لكان فاسداً ؛ لما فيهِ مِنْ لزومِ نقيصة العجزِ وأما سائر الصفاتِ - كالعلم ، والكلام ، والسمع ، والبصر - فلا يصح التخصيص بها (۱) ؛ لأنَّ التخصيص تأثير ، وهذه الصفات ليسَتْ مُؤثرةً في مُتعلقاتِها . وأشار بالعموم في قوله : ( الممكن ( إلى فسادِ مذهب المعتزلة الذينَ خصَّصوا تعلق الإرادة بالخير دونَ الشر ، وبالصلاح والأصلح دون مقابليهما (۱) جواب عن سؤال مقدَّر ؛ تقديره : لِمَ خصصتم القدرة بصفة الإرادة ولم تخصّصوها بالعلم أو الكلام أو السمع أو البصر ؟
Allah the Most High willed the existence of this contingent thing, and did not will that other contingent thing, but rather willed its non-existence. Indeed, that is proof of utmost perfection; for His action, Exalted and Most High, in contingent things is by pure will and choice, and there is no motive upon Him for any contingent thing nor coercion nor compulsion, as He said, Exalted and Most High: 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' [Al-Qasas 68]. And if you said: Allah the Most High has power over this existing contingent thing, and does not have power over its opposite... that would be corrupt; because of the necessary consequence of the deficiency of incapacity. As for the other attributes — such as knowledge, speech, hearing, and sight — specification with them is not valid (1); because specification is an effect, and these attributes are not effective in their objects. And He indicated with the general in His saying: 'the contingent' the corruption of the doctrine of the Mu'tazila who specified the relation of will to good without evil, and to what is beneficial and most beneficial without their opposites. (1) Answer to an implied question: its import: Why did you specify power with the attribute of will and not specify it with knowledge, speech, hearing, or sight?
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the scope of God's will (al-irāda). The author begins by affirming that when God wills one contingent thing to exist and wills another not to exist, this is not a limitation but a sign of perfection. God's actions in contingent things are by pure will and choice without any external motive, coercion, or compulsion. The verse from Surah Al-Qasas is quoted to support this. The author then refutes a possible objection: if someone said that God has power over one existing contingent thing but not its opposite, that would be corrupt because it would imply the deficiency of incapacity (al-ʿajz). The author then explains why specification (al-takhṣīṣ) only applies to the attribute of will and not to other attributes like knowledge, speech, hearing, or sight. Specification is an effect (taʾthīr), and these other attributes are not effective in their objects; they merely perceive or disclose. The passage ends by showing that the general expression 'the contingent' refutes the Mu'tazila who restrict God's will to good and beneficial things only, excluding evil and harm. The footnote clarifies why will is specified with power: because specification involves effect, and only will has that property.

The Attribute of Life and Its Non-Relational Nature
Sanusi defines life and contrasts it with related attributes that require external objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute of life is defined as a condition for perception and how it differs from attributes that require an external object.

Core Idea
Life is the condition for perception, not a relation to an external object.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary defines the divine attribute of life and distinguishes it from other attributes like power, will, and knowledge. Before we explore how life enables perception, we need to understand why it is not classified as a 'related attribute'. This distinction clarifies the unique role of life in the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the author's definition of life and the contrast with related attributes sink in.
The passage includes footnotes; read them for clarity.
2
Identify the examples of related attributes
Notice how power, will, knowledge, speech, hearing, and sight each require an object beyond their subject. List these objects: maqdur, murad, ma'lum, ma'na, masmu', mubsar.
3
Contrast life's requirement
Life does not require an external object; it only needs its own locus. Understand that life is a 'correcting' attribute (musahhihah) for perception, meaning it is a rational condition.
This is the key difference: life enables perception but does not relate to an external object.
4
Check the pronoun clarification
The footnote explains that the pronoun in 'وهي ما يقتضي' refers to the related attributes, not to life. This avoids confusion.
This subtle point ensures the reader doesn't misattribute the requirement.
5
Reflect on the doctrine
Consider why this distinction matters: it shows that God's life is self-sufficient and not dependent on external objects, while other attributes imply relation to creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ صفة الحياة ]
وَالْحَيَاةُ : صِفَةٌ يَصِحُ مِمَّنْ قَامَتْ بِهِ الإِدْرَاكُ (٢)
وهي (۳) ما يقتضي
يعني : أنَّ الحياة ليسَتْ مِنَ الصفاتِ المتعلِّقَةِ ؛ لذاتِهِ زائداً على القيام بمحله ؛ كالقدرةِ ؛ فإنَّها تقتضي زائداً على القيام بمحلها ؛ وهو المقدورُ الذي يتأتّى بها إيجاده وإعدامه ، والإرادة تقتضي لذاتها مراداً يتخصَّصُ بها ، والعلم يقتضي معلوماً ينكشفُ به ، والكلام يقتضي معنى يدلُّ عليه ، والسمعُ يقتضي مسموعاً يُسمَعُ .
والبصر يقتضي مُبصَراً ، والحياة لا تقتضي زائداً على القيام بمحلها وإنَّما هي صفةٌ مُصححة للإدراكِ ؛ بمعنى : أنَّها شرطٌ عقلي له .
(۲) وفي شرح العقيدة الصغرى » ( ص ١٦٥ ) : ( صفة تصحح لمن قامت به أن يتصف بالإدراك ) .
(۳) الضمير يعود على ( الصفات المتعلقة ) ، لا لحد الحياة كما لا يخفى
[ The Attribute of Life ]
And life (al-hayah) is: an attribute by which it is valid for the one in whom it subsists to perceive (al-idrak) (2).
And it is (3) that which requires.
Meaning: that life is not among the related attributes (al-sifat al-muta`alliqah); by its essence, [it requires] nothing beyond subsistence in its locus; like power (al-qudrah): for it requires, beyond subsistence in its locus, the object of power (al-maqdur) which can be brought into existence and annihilated by it; and will (al-iradah) by its essence requires an object of will (al-murad) specified by it; and knowledge (al-`ilm) requires a known (al-ma`lum) disclosed by it; and speech (al-kalam) requires a meaning (al-ma`na) indicated by it; and hearing (al-sam`) requires something heard (al-masmu`); and sight (al-basar) requires something seen (al-mubsar); and life does not require anything beyond subsistence in its locus, but it is an attribute that renders perception correct (musahhihah li al-idrak); meaning: it is a rational condition (shart `aqli) for it.
(2) And in Sharh al-`Aqida al-Sughra (p. 165): (an attribute that makes it valid for the one in whom it subsists to be described with perception).
(3) The pronoun refers to 'the related attributes' (al-sifat al-muta`alliqah), not to 'life', as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of life (al-hayah) and distinguishes it from the 'related' attributes (al-sifat al-muta`alliqah). It first gives the definition: life is an attribute that makes perception (al-idrak) valid for the one who possesses it. Then it explains what the pronoun in the original text ('it') refers to: it refers to life being not one of the related attributes. Related attributes are those that, by their essence, require an object beyond their subject. Examples are given: power requires an object of power (maqdur), will requires an object of will (murad), knowledge requires a known (ma`lum), speech requires a meaning (ma`na), hearing requires something heard (masmu`), and sight requires something seen (mubsar). Life, in contrast, does not require any object beyond the subject in which it subsists; it only requires its own locus. Instead, life is a 'correcting' (musahhihah) attribute for perception, meaning it is a rational condition (shart `aqli) for perception: if life is present, perception is possible; if absent, perception is impossible. A footnote cites another commentary that defines life similarly. Another footnote clarifies that the pronoun in the phrase 'وهي ما يقتضي' refers to the related attributes, not to life itself, to avoid confusion.

Misconception: One might think that life, like other attributes, requires an external object.
Correction: The passage clarifies that life does not require an external object; it is a condition for perception, not a relational attribute.

Checkpoint
Why is life not considered a 'related attribute' (sifah muta`alliqah)?
A) Because it is not a real attribute of God
B) Because it requires an object beyond its subject
C) Because it does not require an object beyond its subject
D) Because it is identical to perception

Key Takeaway
Life is a non-relational attribute that serves as a necessary condition for perception, unlike attributes that require external objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have power, knowledge, will—all related attributes that reach outward. Yet life, the silent condition beneath them all, requires nothing. When was the last time you thanked Allah for just being alive, without attaching any goal or outcome? Can you spend one minute today in pure awareness of life itself, not thinking about what you can do with it?
?
The passage says life 'makes perception valid'. Your entire spiritual journey depends on perception—hearing the Quran, seeing signs, knowing the truth. If life is a rational condition, are you living as if perception is automatic, or are you guarding the source of your ability to perceive? What would change if you began each day acknowledging that your life is the fragile vessel of every divine gift you receive?
?
Right now, you are reading these words. That act of perception is only possible because life subsists in you. But do you live as if life is a self-sufficient, non-relational gift from Allah, or do you constantly entangle it with objects—things you want, achievements you chase? Is it possible that your pursuit of 'related' blessings has made you neglect the very attribute that makes all blessing perceptible? Commit today to a moment of stillness where you do nothing but feel the life in your breath, asking Allah to make it a means of drawing near to Him.

Perceptions as Types of Knowledge
Sanusi states that perceptions are included in knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school considers sensory perceptions to be included within the definition of knowledge.

Core Idea
Perceptions are knowledge because they involve awareness.

Before defining life, Sanusi clarifies a key point: sensory experiences like seeing and hearing are not separate from knowledge; they are included within it. This follows the Ash'ari school. This lesson explores that inclusion and its implications for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic sentence carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp its concise structure. Note the key terms: العلم, الإدراك, السمع, البصر.
The sentence is brief but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the doctrinal reference
Sanusi attributes this view to Shaykh al-Ash'ari. Understand that this is a specific theological position about the scope of knowledge.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about why including perceptions in knowledge matters for later discussions of attributes like life. It shows that knowledge is broader than intellectual cognition.
This sets the stage for life as a condition for perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدخل في العلم على مقتضى هذا التعريف : إدراك السمع والبصر وسائر الإدراكاتِ ، فهي إذا أنواعٌ مِنَ العلم ، وهذا مذهب الشيخ الأشعري رضيَ الله تعالى عنه
صفة الحياة
وَالْحَيَاةُ : صِفَةٌ يَصِحُ مِمَّنْ قَامَتْ بِهِ الإِدْرَاكُ وهي ما يقتضي يعني : أنَّ الحياة ليسَتْ مِنَ الصفاتِ المتعلِّقَةِ لذاتِهِ زائداً على القيام بمحله؛ كالقدرةِ؛ فإنَّها تقتضي زائداً على القيام بمحلها؛ وهو المقدورُ الذي يتأتّى بها إيجاده وإعدامه، والإرادة تقتضي لذاتها مراداً يتخصَّصُ بها، والعلم يقتضي معلوماً ينكشفُ به، والكلام يقتضي معنى يدلُّ عليه، والسمعُ يقتضي مسموعاً يُسمَعُ، والبصر يقتضي مُبصَراً، والحياة لا تقتضي زائداً على القيام بمحلها وإنَّما هي صفةٌ مُصححة للإدراكِ؛ بمعنى: أنَّها شرطٌ عقلي له، يلزمُ من عدمها عدمُ الإدراكِ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِها وجودُ الإدراكِ ولا عدمه، وبالله تعالى التوفيق
And there enters into knowledge according to the requirement of this definition: the perception of hearing and sight and all other perceptions, so they are thus types of knowledge, and this is the doctrine of Shaykh al-Ash'ari, may Allah Most High be pleased with him. The attribute of life. And life is an attribute by which he in whom it subsists becomes valid for perception, and it is what requires. That is to say: life is not among the attributes that require something additional beyond subsisting in its subject; like power (qudra), for it requires something additional beyond subsisting in its subject—namely, the potential object (maqdur) by which its bringing into existence and annihilation are achieved—and will (irada) requires by its essence a willed object (murad) that is particularized by it, and knowledge (ilm) requires a known object (ma'lum) that is disclosed by it, and speech (kalam) requires a meaning that it indicates, and hearing (sam') requires a heard object (masmu') that is heard, and sight (basar) requires a seen object (mubsar), while life does not require anything additional beyond subsisting in its subject; rather it is an attribute that makes perception sound, meaning that it is a rational condition for it: from its absence necessarily follows the absence of perception, but from its existence neither the existence of perception nor its absence necessarily follows, and with Allah Most High is success.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that on a certain definition of knowledge, sensory perceptions (hearing, sight, etc.) are included as types of knowledge, following the Ash'ari position. It then moves to the main topic: defining the attribute of life (al-hayat). The definition states that life is an attribute that makes the being in whom it subsists capable of perception. The author then clarifies that life is not a 'relational' attribute (sifah muta'alliqah) that requires an external object beyond its subject. He contrasts life with other attributes: power requires a potential object, will requires a willed object, knowledge requires a known object, speech requires a meaning, hearing requires a heard object, and sight requires a seen object. Life, however, requires nothing beyond the subject itself; it is merely a 'correcting' (musahhihah) attribute, meaning a rational condition for perception. The existence of life does not necessitate perception (since other conditions are needed), but its absence necessitates the absence of perception. The passage ends with an invocation of divine assistance.

الإدراك

A) They are separate faculties from knowledge.

D) They require the attribute of life.

Sensory perceptions are included in knowledge according to the Ash'ari position.

?

?
You now know that according to this lesson, your perceptions are not neutral sensations but types of knowledge. How does this change your responsibility when you witness injustice or hear backbiting? What will you do with that knowledge?
?
This lesson says that even your physical senses generate knowledge that your Lord holds you accountable for. Are you living as if every perception matters to Allah? What one perception today will you treat as sacred knowledge?

Life as a Non-Relational Attribute
Sanusi defines life and contrasts it with other attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defines the attribute of life, why it is not a relational attribute, and how it contrasts with power, will, knowledge, speech, hearing, and sight.

Core Idea
Life is a condition for perception, not a relation to an object.

After establishing that perceptions are knowledge, Sanusi defines life. He shows that life is unique among attributes because it requires nothing beyond the subject itself; it is a condition, not a relation. This lesson walks through the definition and the contrast with six other attributes that are relational.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of life
Read the first part of the Arabic passage: 'وَالْحَيَاةُ : صِفَةٌ يَصِحُ مِمَّنْ قَامَتْ بِهِ الإِدْرَاكُ'. Understand that life is defined as an attribute that makes its subject valid for perception.
2
Identify the six relational attributes
Sanusi lists six attributes that require an external object: power, will, knowledge, speech, hearing, and sight. Find each one and note what object it requires (e.g., power requires a maqdur).
These are called الصفات المتعلقة (relational attributes).
3
Understand why life is non-relational
Contrast with the previous list: life does not require any object beyond its subject. It is a صفة مصححة (corrective attribute).
4
Grasp life as a rational condition
Sanusi explains that life is a شرط عقلي (rational condition) for perception: without life, perception cannot occur; but with life, perception may or may not occur depending on other factors.
This shows life is necessary but not sufficient for perception.

Source Text

Defining Eternal Hearing, Sight, and Perception
Sanusi defines eternal hearing, sight, and perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of eternal hearing, sight, and perception as attributes that disclose every existent with a distinctive disclosure.

Core Idea
Eternal hearing, sight, and perception are universal, not limited like human senses.

Sanusi begins by defining three divine attributes: hearing, sight, and perception. He emphasizes that these attributes are not like our senses; they disclose every existent in a unique way. This foundational definition sets the stage for understanding how these attributes relate to everything, including God's own essence and created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition for hearing
Read how Sanusi defines eternal hearing: 'an attribute by which every existing thing is disclosed as it is, with a disclosure necessarily distinct from other disclosures.' Notice the universality and the insistence on a unique mode of disclosure.
Hearing is not about sound waves; it's about disclosing existence.
2
Sight and perception are similar
Sanusi says sight is like hearing, and perception (for those who affirm it) is like them. Understand that these attributes share the same definitional structure.
Perception (idrak) is a contested attribute; Sanusi includes it conditionally.
3
Contrast with human senses
Reflect: in our world, hearing and sight are limited to certain objects (sounds, colors). But for God, there is no such limitation. This will be developed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفة السمع والبصر، والإدراك على القول بها
وَالسَّمْعُ الأَزَلِيُّ : صِفَةٌ يَنْكَشِفُ بِهَا كُلُّ مَوْجُودٍ عَلَى مَا هُوَ بِهِ أَنْكِشَافاً يُبَايِنُ سِوَاهُ ضَرُورَةٌ ، وَالْبَصَرُ مِثْلُهُ ، وَالْإِدْرَاكُ عَلَى الْقَوْلِ بِهِ مِثْلُهُمَا
هذه الصفات مشتركة في تعلقها بالموجود، قديماً كان أو حادثاً، إلا أنَّها في الشاهدِ مُختصَّةٌ ببعض الموجوداتِ؛ لتخصيصه تعالى لها بذلك، ولو خرق سبحانه وتعالى العادة في ذلك لصح أنْ تتعلَّق بسائرِ الموجوداتِ، ولهذا جازَتْ رؤية المخلوقِ لمولانا تبارك وتعالى على مذهب أهل الحقِّ، وجازَ سماعُهم لكلامِهِ القديم القائم بذاتِهِ العليَّةِ جل وعلا، مع أن الرؤية في الشاهدِ إنَّما جرَتِ العادةُ بتعلقها بالأجرام وألوانها وأكوانها، والسمعَ في الشاهدِ إِنَّما جرَتِ العادةُ بتعلقِهِ بالحروف والأصوات
ولما استحال دخول التخصيص في صفات المولى تبارك وتعالى؛ لاستلزامِهِ الافتقار إلى المخصّص المستلزم للحدوثِ . . وجب تعميم تعلق صفاتِهِ تعالى بكلِّ ما تصلحُ له؛ لأنَّها واجبةٌ، فلا يمكنُ أنْ
The attribute of hearing, sight, and perception according to the view affirming it. And eternal hearing (al-sam' al-azali) is an attribute by which every existing thing is disclosed as it is, with a disclosure necessarily distinct from other disclosures, and sight (al-basar) is like it, and perception (al-idrak), according to the view affirming it, is like them. These attributes share in their relation to the existent, whether eternal or originated, except that in the witnessed world they are specific to some existents due to His, exalted, particularization of them for that. And had He, glorified and exalted, broken the norm in that, they could relate to all existents. Hence, the vision of the creature of our Lord, blessed and exalted, is permissible according to the doctrine of the People of Truth, and their hearing of His eternal speech, subsisting in His exalted essence, is permissible, even though vision in the witnessed world is customarily related to bodies, their colors, and their accidents, and hearing in the witnessed world is customarily related to letters and sounds. And since the entry of particularization into the attributes of our Lord, blessed and exalted, is impossible because it would entail need of a particularizer, which entails originatedness... it became necessary to universalize the relation of His attributes, exalted, to everything that is suitable for them, because they are necessary, so it is not possible that...
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines the eternal attributes of hearing (al-sam'), sight (al-basar), and perception (al-idrak, only for those who affirm it). Hearing is defined as an attribute that discloses every existent as it is, with a disclosure necessarily distinct from others; sight and perception are similar. The author then explains that these attributes, in principle, relate to all existents (both eternal and originated), but in the witnessed world (al-shahid) they are customarily limited to specific types of objects due to God's particularization. However, if God were to break the norm, they could relate to any existent. This provides the basis for the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife (ru'yat Allah) and hearing His speech (sam' kalam Allah) according to Ahl al-Sunna. The passage concludes with a kalam argument: since particularization (takhsis) cannot apply to God's attributes (because it would imply need and contingency), the relation of His attributes must be universal to everything that can receive them, because the attributes are necessary (wajiba). The text cuts off but the argument is clear: God's hearing and sight are not limited like human senses.

السمع الأزلي
البصر
الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only sounds
B) Every existing thing
C) Only heavenly matters
D) Only what is audible

Key Takeaway
Eternal hearing, sight, and perception are defined as attributes that disclose every existent with a distinct mode of disclosure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God's hearing discloses every existent as it is. When you speak, do you remember that God hears not only your words but the reality of your heart's intention? How will you change the way you speak this week to align with that reality?
?
God's sight discloses every existent as it is. What are you hiding in your life that you hope no one sees? Confess to yourself that God sees it already. What will you do today to bring that hidden thing into the light of His sight?
?
Perception (idrak) for God is distinct from any human perception. Where have you assumed God does not care or notice because your situation seems insignificant? Repent from limiting God's perception and trust that He sees every detail of your life.

Divine Attributes

The Universal Relation of God's Attributes
Sanusi argues for the universal relation of hearing, sight, and perception, and against particularization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's attributes of hearing, sight, and perception must relate universally to all existents, and why particularization is impossible for Divine attributes.

Core Idea
God's attributes are necessary, so they cannot be limited by particularization.

After defining the attributes, Sanusi tackles a critical theological point: these attributes in the witnessed world (al-shahid) are customarily restricted to certain objects, but that custom does not apply to God. He argues that since God's attributes are necessary (wajiba), they cannot be subject to particularization (takhsis), which would imply need and contingency. This reasoning establishes the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife and hearing His eternal speech.

Guided Path
1
Understand the shared relation
Sanusi says these attributes share a relation (ta'allaq) to all existents, whether eternal or originated. In the witnessed world, they are customarily specific, but that is due to God's particularization.
The 'witnessed world' is the world we perceive; God can break norms.
2
The argument from impossibility of particularization
Sanusi argues that particularization (takhsis) cannot enter God's attributes because it would entail need for a particularizer (mukhassis), which implies originatedness (huduth). Since God's attributes are eternal and necessary, they must relate to everything suitable.
This is a standard kalam argument: anything that requires a particularizer is contingent.
3
Connect to the possibility of seeing and hearing God
Note the conclusion: because of this universal relation, it is permissible (ja'iz) for creatures to see God (ru'ya) and hear His speech (sam' kalamihi) according to Ahl al-Sunna, even though in the witnessed world sight and hearing are customarily tied to bodies and sounds.
This is the practical payoff of the theological argument.

Source Text

The author then explains that these attributes, in principle, relate to all existents (both eternal and originated), but in the witnessed world (al-shahid) they are customarily limited to specific types of objects due to God's particularization. However, if God were to break the norm, they could relate to any existent. This provides the basis for the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife (ru'yat Allah) and hearing His speech (sam' kalam Allah) according to Ahl al-Sunna. The passage concludes with a kalam argument: since particularization (takhsis) cannot apply to God's attributes (because it would imply need and contingency), the relation of His attributes must be universal to everything that can receive them, because the attributes are necessary (wajiba). The text cuts off but the argument is clear: God's hearing and sight are not limited like human senses.

Key Terms
التعلق
التخصيص
الرؤية
سماع كلام الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's hearing and sight are limited like human senses, only perceiving sounds and visible things.
Correction: Sanusi argues that God's attributes are necessary and cannot be particularized; they relate to all existents universally, and the customary limits in the witnessed world do not apply to God's essence.

Checkpoint
Why is particularization (takhsis) impossible for God's attributes?
A) Because God's attributes are eternal and necessary, so they cannot need a particularizer.
B) Because God is too great to be described.
C) Because particularization only applies to created things.
D) Because the Quran says so.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of hearing, sight, and perception relate universally to all existents because they are necessary and cannot be limited by particularization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty that seems beyond your control, do you truly believe that God's attribute of hearing encompasses every whisper of your heart, or do you act as if He is distant? Will you, from this moment, speak to Him with the certainty that He hears you perfectly?
?
The passage teaches that God's attributes cannot be particularized. Reflect: Are there areas of your life where you have particularized God's mercy or power—believing He cannot reach into that sin, that pain, that failure? What would change if you truly embraced His universal attributes?
?
You have just learned that God's sight is not limited to bodies and colors. How does this truth confront your attempts to hide parts of your life from Him? Will you now live with the constant awareness that He sees your inner state as clearly as your outer actions?

The Rule of Divine Attributes and the Consensus on Sight
Sanusi states the rule on divine attributes and the consensus on sight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental rule that essential attributes are obligatory for God and the consensus that divine sight attaches to every existent.

Core Idea
For God, essential attributes are necessary, not contingent; all agree His sight encompasses all existents.

This lesson establishes two foundational principles: the logical rule that essential attributes are necessary for God, and the unanimous agreement that divine sight is universal. These set the stage for the debate on other divine perceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the concise style of kalam.
2
Identify the rule about divine attributes
Find the sentence stating that everything God accepts from essential attributes is obligatory because contingent attributes are impossible for Him.
This is a key logical principle.
3
Understand the consensus on sight
Note that all People of Truth agree that God's sight (basar) can attach to every existent.
This consensus is the starting point.
4
Reflect on the disagreement
The passage then introduces a split on other perceptions—observe how this builds on the consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتصف بما يقتضي حدوثها، والقاعدة : أن كل ما يقبله تعالى من الصفات الذاتية وكمالاتها . . فهو واجب له؛ لاستحالة اتصافه تعالى بالجائزات. وقد اتفق أهل الحق قاطبة على جواز تعلق البصر بكل موجود، واختلفوا في جواز تعلق ما عدا الرؤية من الإدراكات بكل موجود: فذهب القدماء منهم؛ كعبد الله بن سعيد الكلابي والقلانسي: إلى أن هذا العموم مختص بالرؤية، وبقية الإدراكات لا يجوز أن تعم الموجودات. ونقل عن إمام أهل السنة وشيخهم الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري مخالفتهما في ذلك، وصار إلى جواز عموم كل إدراك لكل موجود. ونقل عن عبد الله بن سعيد: أنه لما خص تعلق السمع بالأصوات.. ذهب إلى أن الكلام الأزلي لا يصح أن يسمع؛ يعني والله تعالى أعلم: بل يدرك بصفة العلم، وفي قوله ذلك مخالفة لقواطع السمع. والشيخ أبو الحسن رضي الله تعالى عنه لما قال: إدراك السمع يعم كل موجود.. جوز تعلقه بكلام الله تعالى، وقال بوقوع ذلك الجائز على ما ورد به السمع في حق موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام. كل موجود يصح أن يرى. وعمدة الشيخ في ذلك: ما ثبت في فصل الرؤية من أن الوجود هو المصرح للرؤية؛ بمعنى: أنه متعلقها فلا فرق بين موجود وموجود؛ فإذا رئي موجود أو أدرك بغير الرؤية . . جاز تعلقهما بكل موجود. وقد اختلف الأصحاب في الأكوان التي هي متعلق الرؤية في وقتنا.
It is described by what necessitates their temporality, and the rule: that everything that the Most High accepts from the essential attributes and their perfections is obligatory for Him, because it is impossible for the Most High to be described with contingent things. And the People of Truth have all agreed on the permissibility of the attachment of sight to every existent, and they disagreed on the permissibility of the attachment of perceptions other than vision to every existent. The earlier ones among them, such as Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Kullabi and al-Qalanisi, held that this generality is specific to vision, and the rest of the perceptions are not permitted to encompass existents. And it is reported from the Imam of the Ahl al-Sunna and their master, Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, that he disagreed with them in that, and he held the permissibility of the generality of every perception for every existent. And it is reported from Abdullah ibn Sa'id: that when he specified the attachment of hearing to sounds, he held that eternal speech cannot be heard; meaning, and God knows best, rather it is perceived by the attribute of knowledge, and in that statement of his there is opposition to definitive texts of revelation. And Sheikh Abu al-Hasan, may God be pleased with him, when he said: the perception of hearing encompasses every existent, he permitted its attachment to the speech of God the Most High, and he said that this contingent thing occurred as reported in revelation regarding Moses, peace be upon him. Every existent can be seen. And the basis of the Sheikh in that is what is established in the chapter on vision: that existence is the qualifier for vision; meaning that it is its object, so there is no difference between one existent and another. So if an existent is seen or perceived by other than vision, it is permissible for them to attach to every existent. And the companions differed about the akwan (states) that are the object of vision in our time.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents a theological rule about divine attributes and then discusses an important disagreement among early Sunni theologians regarding the scope of divine perception (idrak). The author begins by stating a general rule: anything that the Most High can accept from the essential attributes and their perfections is obligatory for Him, because it is impossible for God to be described with contingent (ja'iz) attributes. Then he notes that all People of Truth agree that God's sight (basar) can attach to every existent. However, they disagree on whether other perceptions (like hearing, smell, etc.) also attach to every existent. The earlier scholars, such as Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Kullabi and al-Qalanisi, held that this generality is specific to vision only; other perceptions do not generalize to all existents. In contrast, Imam al-Ash'ari is reported to have disagreed and held that every perception is general to every existent. The author then mentions a specific consequence of Ibn Kullab's position: because Ibn Kullab restricted hearing to sounds, he argued that God's eternal speech (al-kalam al-azali) cannot be heard; rather it is perceived by the attribute of knowledge. The author notes that this contradicts definitive texts (qawati' al-sam'). The passage then explains al-Ash'ari's view: since hearing encompasses every existent, it can attach to God's speech, and this actually occurred for Moses (peace be upon him). The author states that everything existent can be seen. The proof for al-Ash'ari's position is based on the principle from the chapter on vision: existence itself makes something perceptible; there is no difference between existents in this regard. So if an existent is seen or perceived by another sense, that perception can attach to any existent. Finally, the author mentions a later disagreement among the companions (the Asha'ira) about the 'akwan' (states or accidents) that are the object of vision in our time. This passage is a careful exposition of a nuanced debate, showing the reasoning behind each position and the scriptural basis for the Ash'ari stance.

Key Terms
البصر (basar)
الصفات الذاتية (al-sifat al-dhatiyya)
الجائز (al-ja'iz)
الوجود (al-wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any attribute of God could be either necessary or contingent.
Correction: The rule states that essential attributes are obligatory, not contingent; contingency is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What does the rule state about essential attributes of God?
A) They are contingent and possible
B) They are obligatory because contingency is impossible for God
C) They are only for perfections
D) They are agreed upon by all scholars

Key Takeaway
The rule that essential attributes are necessary for God and the consensus on divine sight are foundational for the debate on other perceptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that God's mercy works exactly like human mercy? How does the rule that God's attributes are necessary (not contingent) challenge that assumption? What must you change in how you think about God?
?
The early scholars restricted God's perception to sight alone. Are there areas where you have limited God's attributes based on your own experience? Write down one assumption about God that you need to surrender today.
?
The rule says that essential attributes are obligatory for God. Does your daily life reflect a deep conviction that God is necessarily all-seeing? How would you act differently if you truly believed His sight is not just possible but necessary?

Al-Ash'ari's View on the Generality of Perceptions and Hearing God's Speech
Sanusi reports al-Ash'ari's position and his proof from the nature of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Ash'ari held that all perceptions attach to every existent, and how this applies to hearing God's speech.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari argued that existence itself qualifies a thing for perception, so any existent can be perceived by any sense.

This lesson examines the core argument behind al-Ash'ari's position on the generality of perceptions, particularly the audibility of God's speech. It contrasts his view with that of Ibn Kullab and provides the proof from the nature of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation

Notice the discussion about hearing God's speech.
2
Compare the two positions

Al-Ash'ari's view aligns with the story of Moses.
3
Analyze the proof from existence
The passage states that existence is the qualifier for perception; there is no difference between existents.
This is a key metaphysical claim.
4
Reflect on the later disagreement
The passage ends with a note that companions differed about the akwan (states) as objects of vision.

Hearing Encompasses All Existence and the Hearing of God's Speech
Sanusi discusses the scope of hearing and whether it can perceive God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument for why hearing can perceive God's eternal speech and the scholarly differences on this issue.

Core Idea
Hearing, like vision, perceives any existent—including God’s eternal speech.

Sanusi tackles a debated question: can hearing perceive God’s eternal speech? He builds on the principle that existence is the prerequisite for perception, arguing that hearing, like vision, can attach to any existent. This lesson explores his reasoning and the scholarly context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the passage presents an argument about the scope of hearing.
The passage references earlier discussions on vision.
2
Identify key premise and conclusion
The premise is that hearing encompasses every existent. The conclusion is that hearing can perceive God's speech.
The passage cites Shaykh Abu al-Hasan.
3
Understand the analogy with vision
The passage refers to the chapter on vision where existence is the prerequisite. So if any existent can be seen, any existent can be heard.
This is a key principle in kalam.
4
Note scholarly disagreements
Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and al-Maturidi held that hearing requires sound, so God's speech (eternal and not sound) cannot be heard. The author sides with al-Ghazali, Ibn Kullab, and al-Qalanisi.
This is an ijtihadi matter.
5
Summarize the author's position
The author affirms that hearing can perceive God's speech without implying that God's speech is sound, preserving the attribute of eternal speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشيخ أبو الحسنِ رضيَ اللهُ تعالى عنهُ لمَّا قالَ : إدراك السمعِ يعمُّ كل موجود.. جوَّزَ تعلَّقَهُ بكلامِ اللهِ تعالى ، وقالَ بكلام الله تعالى ، وقال بوقوع ذلك الجائز على ما ورد بهِ السمعُ في حقٌّ موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام (١) كلُّ موجود يصح أن يُرى ] وعمدة الشيخ في ذلك : ما ثبت في فصل الرؤيةِ مِنْ أنَّ الوجود هو المصرِّحُ للرؤيةِ ؛ بمعنى : أَنَّهُ مُتعلّقها فلا فرق بين موجود وموجود ; فإذا رئي موجود أو أُدرِكَ بغير الرؤيةِ . . جازَ تعلقهما بكل موجود (٢) وقد اختلف الأصحابُ في الأكوان التي هي مُتعلَّقُ الرؤية في وقتنا من تقدمه أبو إسحاق ـ يعني : الإسفرايني - باختياره هذا المذهب حتى ادعى أن جميع من متكلمي أهل الحديث على هذا ، واتفقوا أن لا يمكن سماع ما ليس بصوت ) ، وهو قول إمام الهدى أبي منصور الماتريدي في كتابه التوحيد » ( ص ٥٩ ) ، وبهذا تعلم أن المسألة اجتهادية ، واختيار المصنف هو ما ذهب إليه حجة الإسلام الغزالي في «الاقتصاد » ( ص ٢٥٨ ) ، وما ذهب إليه كلٌّ من الإمام ابن كُلاب والإمام القلانسي . . لا يقضي بنفي صفة الكلام القديم، بخلاف المعتزلة الذي منعوا السماع لانتفاء الكلام النفسي من أصله .
And the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan (may Allah be pleased with him) when he said: 'The perception of hearing encompasses every existent', permitted its attachment to the speech of Allah Almighty, and he affirmed the speech of Allah Almighty, and he affirmed the occurrence of that permissible thing according to what hearing has reported concerning Moses (peace be upon him). (1) 'Every existent is valid to be seen.' And the proof of the Shaykh in that is what has been established in the chapter of vision: that existence is the prerequisite for vision, meaning that it is its object, so there is no difference between one existent and another; if an existent is seen or perceived by other than sight, then the attachment of both is permissible to every existent. (2) And the companions have differed concerning the 'akwan' that are the object of vision in our time. Abu Ishaq—meaning al-Isfarayini—chose this school and even claimed that all the theologians of the People of Hadith are upon this, and they agreed that one cannot hear what is not sound, and it is the saying of Imam al-Huda Abu Mansur al-Maturidi in his book 'al-Tawhid' (p. 59); and by this you know that the issue is ijtihadi, and the choice of the author is what Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali went to in 'al-Iqtisad' (p. 258), and what each of Imam Ibn Kullab and Imam al-Qalanisi went to does not necessitate the negation of the attribute of pre-eternal speech, contrary to the Mu'tazila who prevented hearing due to the negation of internal speech from its origin.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents an argument concerning the scope of auditory perception. It begins by citing Shaykh Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) who stated that hearing encompasses every existent. Based on this premise, he permitted the possibility that hearing could attach to God's speech, which is an eternal attribute. He also affirmed that this permissible event actually occurred as reported in revelation, namely that Moses heard God's speech. The text then provides the ground for this view: in the chapter on vision it is established that existence itself is the prerequisite for being perceived—so any existent can be seen or heard. Therefore, hearing, like vision, can in principle attach to any existent, including God's speech. The author then notes that the companions (Ash'ari scholars) differed over the 'akwan' (accidents of motion, rest, etc.) as objects of vision in our time—this is a separate issue but related to the general principle. He reports that Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini claimed that all theologians of the People of Hadith agreed that sound is necessary for hearing, meaning that one cannot hear what is not sound. Al-Maturidi held the same view in his book al-Tawhid. The author concludes that this is an ijtihadi matter, and that his own chosen position is that of al-Ghazali, Ibn Kullab, and al-Qalanisi, which does not negate the attribute of pre-eternal speech, unlike the Mu'tazila who rejected hearing God's speech because they deny internal speech altogether.

Key Terms
إدراك السمع
الرؤية
كلام الله
الكلام القديم
الكلام النفسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hearing God's speech implies His speech is sound or letters.
Correction: The passage shows that hearing can perceive God's eternal speech without it being sound, because existence itself is the condition for perception.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for saying that hearing can perceive God's speech?
A) Because hearing is stronger than vision.
B) Because existence itself is the prerequisite for any perception, so hearing can attach to any existent.
C) Because God's speech is a sound that can be heard.
D) Because the Prophet Moses reported it directly.

Key Takeaway
Hearing can perceive God's eternal speech because existence is the condition for perception, regardless of sound or letter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or listen to the Qur'an, do you genuinely perceive it as the eternal speech of God, or do you reduce it to mere sounds and words? What would change in your heart if you truly believed you are hearing God's attribute?
?
The Mu'tazila denied hearing God's speech because they denied internal speech. Is there any theological assumption you hold that might prevent you from fully embracing God's attributes as real and perceptible? Examine your own beliefs honestly.
?
If hearing can perceive God's speech, then every time you hear the Qur'an, you are encountering God's eternal attribute. How does this affect your reverence and attention during recitation? Are you willing to change your practice to reflect this reality?

Defending the Multiplicity of Divine Attributes
Sanusi presents an objection about divine perfections and knowledge and answers by distinguishing their natures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the multiplicity of divine attributes (like sight and knowledge) against the charge of redundancy, by distinguishing the distinct realities of their attachments.

Core Idea
Multiple divine attributes can attach to the same object without redundancy—each has a unique reality of disclosure.

This passage presents a classic kalam objection: if God's knowledge and perceptions (like sight) both encompass all existents, doesn't that lead to redundancy? Sanusi responds by showing that each attribute has its own distinct 'reality of attachment,' so they can coexist without conflict. Understanding this answer is key to appreciating how Sunni theology accounts for the diversity of divine perfections.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the two problematic scenarios the objector raises: either the attachments differ (leading to tahsil al-hasil or ijtima' al-mithlayn) or they don't (leading to concealment).
Note the use of technical terms like 'tahsil al-hasil'.
2
Understand the answer's first move
Sanusi chooses the first horn: they attach to the same object. Then see why he says this doesn't cause redundancy.
3
Examine the core reasoning
The key point: the perceptions and knowledge have different realities (haqa'iq), so their attachments, though to the same object, are distinct. Each has a unique 'reality of disclosure'.
This is the central insight.
4
Connect to the analogy of power and will
Sanusi reminds us of the earlier example: qudra and irada both attach to possibles but are distinct attributes.
This reinforces the same principle.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this argument safeguards the multiplicity of God's attributes without implying composition or multiplicity in His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : إذا وجبَ تعلُّق هذهِ الإدراكات في حقه تعالى بكل موجود (٤) ، والعلمُ أيضاً قد تعلَّق بها (٥) ، فيلزم إمَّا تحصيل الحاصل ، أو اجتماعُ المثلينِ (۱) إنْ كانَ ما تعلَّقَتْ بهِ غيرَ ما تعلَّقَ بهِ العلم ، وإمَّا خفاء بعض المعلوماتِ عنِ العلمِ إِنْ كَانَ ما تعلَّقَتْ بهِ تلكَ الإدراكات لم يتعلَّق بهِ العلم (۲) ، وكلا الأمرين مستحيل . قلتُ : نختارُ مِنَ القسمينِ الأَوَّلَ ؛ وهو أنَّ ما تعلَّقَتْ بهِ تلكَ الإدراكات هو عين ما تعلَّق بهِ العلم ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ ذلكَ تحصيل الحاصل ولا اجتماع المثلين ؛ وذلك أنَّ هذه الإدراكاتِ لمَّا كانَتْ غير متحدة الحقيقة ، سواء قلنا : إنَّها أنواع العلم أو لا(۳) ، فتعلقاتُها كذلك غيرُ متَّحدةٍ ، فاجتماع تعلقاتِها في متعلَّق واحد ليسَ مِنْ تحصيل الحاصل ، ولا مِن اجتماع الأمثالِ ، بل كلُّ متعلق منها له حقيقةٌ مِنَ الانكشافِ تخصُّهُ ليسَتْ عين حقيقة سواه ، وكلُّ حقيقةٍ منها عامَّةٌ لما تصح لهُ . وهذا كما تقدَّمَ : أنَّ متعلَّق القدرة والإرادة واحد ؛ وهـو الممكناتُ ، ولا يلزم من اجتماعِهما في متعلق واحد تحصيل الحاصل ؛ لاختلافِ حقيقتي تعلقهما ، وكلٌّ منهما عام بتعلَّقِهِ الخاص بحقيقته لجميع الممكناتِ ، ولهذا أشرنا بقولنا : ( يباين سواه ضرورة ) .
If you say: Since these perceptions (idrakat) necessarily attach in His right to every existent (4), and knowledge also attaches to them (5), then either acquisition of what is already acquired (tahsil al-hasil) or congregation of similars (ijtima' al-mithlayn) follows (1) if what they attach to is other than what knowledge attaches to, or else some known things become hidden from knowledge if what those perceptions attach to is not attached by knowledge (2) — and both possibilities are impossible. I say: We choose the first of the two divisions, namely that what those perceptions attach to is identical to what knowledge attaches to, and that does not entail acquisition of what is already acquired nor congregation of similars. That is because these perceptions, since they are not identical in reality, whether we say they are types of knowledge or not (3), their attachments are likewise non-identical. So the congregation of their attachments upon a single object is neither acquisition of what is already acquired nor from congregation of similars; rather each attachment has a reality of disclosure that is specific to it, not identical to the reality of another. And each reality is general for what it can attach to. This is as we have already mentioned: that the object of power and will is one, namely possibles, and their congregation upon a single object does not entail acquisition of what is already acquired due to the difference of the realities of their attachments, and each is general with its specific attachment according to its reality for all possibles, and for this we indicated by our saying: 'it necessarily differs from others'.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (fa-in qulta) and answer (qultu) concerning the divine attributes of perception (idrakat) such as sight and hearing, and their relation to knowledge. The objector argues: if these perceptions attach to every existent, and knowledge also attaches to every existent, then there are three problems: (a) if they attach to different things, then either the thing perceived is already known (tahsil al-hasil) or two similar attachments coincide (ijtima' al-mithlayn); (b) if they attach to different things, then some things are not known by knowledge, which is impossible. The answer chooses the first horn: they attach to the same object as knowledge. But this does not entail redundancy because the perceptions have different realities (haqiqa) from each other and from knowledge. Each perception has a unique 'reality of disclosure' (haqiqa min al-inkishaf). Hence their attachments are distinct, even if to the same object. The analogy of power (qudra) and will (irada) is cited: they both attach to the same possibles, but their attachments differ in reality, so no redundancy. The core point is that diversity of attributes does not entail multiplicity of objects; the same object can be related to multiple distinct attributes.

Key Terms
الإدراكات (perceptions)
التعلّق (attachment)
تحصيل الحاصل (acquisition of what is acquired)
اجتماع المثلين (congregation of similars)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the attributes of sight and hearing would be redundant with knowledge because they all encompass all existents.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that each attribute has a distinct reality (haqiqa) and thus its attachment is unique, so no redundancy occurs.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does the attachment of perceptions and knowledge to the same object not entail tahsil al-hasil?
A) Because the object is different
B) Because the attachments have different realities
C) Because knowledge does not exist
D) Because perceptions are not real attributes

Key Takeaway
The divine attributes have distinct realities, so they can all attach to the same object without redundancy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ponder that Allah's knowledge, sight, and hearing all encompass you completely yet with distinct disclosures, do you feel the weight of being fully known and seen? How does that change the way you speak and act when you are alone?
?
Consider one area of your life where you have focused only on Allah's knowledge but neglected His other attributes. What practical step can you take this week to remember that He also sees and hears that very matter with a unique reality?
?
If Allah's attributes are not redundant, then each one demands a specific response from you. Which attribute of Allah do you find most challenging to live by? Why, and what will you do to honor that attribute starting today?

Multiple Perceptions and the Same Object: No Redundancy
Sanusi refutes that multiple perceptions on one object entail redundancy or contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attachment of multiple divine perceptions (such as hearing and sight) to the same object as knowledge does not entail logical redundancy (تحصيل الحاصل) or contradiction, and how Sanusi defends this using the distinct realities of disclosure.

Core Idea
Multiple perceptions of the same object are not redundant; each has a unique mode of disclosure.

Sanusi tackles a potential objection: if Allah knows all things, why attribute additional perceptions like hearing and sight? Critics claim this leads to 'obtaining the already obtained' or 'conjunction of likes'. In this passage, he refutes this by showing that each perception has a distinct reality of disclosure, illustrated by the analogy of power and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its English translation to get a general sense of the argument.
Notice the footnote markers; they clarify terms but are not essential for the main point.
2
Identify the two initial scenarios
Sanusi presents two possibilities for what happens when perceptions attach to the same object as knowledge. Find them in the passage.
One scenario involves different objects, the other involves concealment of some known things.
3
Understand his selection
He chooses the first scenario and argues that even when perceptions attach to the exact same object as knowledge, no redundancy follows.
The key is that perceptions are not identical in reality.
4
Analyze the refutation
Each perception has its own unique reality of disclosure (انكشاف), so their attachments differ even if the object is one. This prevents تحصيل الحاصل and اجتماع المثلين.
5
Consider the analogy
Sanusi compares this to power and will, which both relate to contingent things but differ in the reality of their attachment. Reflect on how this supports the argument.
This analogy reinforces the principle: different attributes can share an object without redundancy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحاصل ، أو اجتماعُ المثلينِ (١) إنْ كانَ ما تعلَّقَتْ بهِ غيرَ ما تعلَّقَ بهِ العلم ، وإمَّا خفاء بعض المعلوماتِ عنِ العلمِ إِنْ كَانَ ما تعلَّقَتْ بهِ تلكَ الإدراكات لم يتعلَّق بهِ العلم (٢) ، وكلا الأمرين مستحيل
قلتُ : نختارُ مِنَ القسمينِ الأَوَّلَ ؛ وهو أنَّ ما تعلَّقَتْ بهِ تلكَ الإدراكات هو عين ما تعلَّق بهِ العلم ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ ذلكَ تحصيل الحاصل ولا اجتماع المثلين ؛ وذلك أنَّ هذه الإدراكاتِ لمَّا كانَتْ غير متحدة الحقيقة ، سواء قلنا : إنَّها أنواع العلم أو لا(٣) ، فتعلقاتُها كذلك غيرُ متَّحدةٍ ، فاجتماع تعلقاتِها في متعلَّق واحد ليسَ مِنْ تحصيل الحاصل ، ولا مِن اجتماع الأمثالِ ، بل كلُّ متعلق منها له حقيقةٌ مِنَ الانكشافِ تخصُّهُ ليسَتْ عين حقيقة سواه ، وكلُّ حقيقةٍ منها عامَّةٌ لما تصح لهُ
وهذا كما تقدَّمَ : أنَّ متعلَّق القدرة والإرادة واحد ؛ وهـو الممكناتُ ، ولا يلزم من اجتماعِهما في متعلق واحد تحصيل الحاصل ؛ لاختلافِ حقيقتي تعلقهما ، وكلٌّ منهما عام بتعلَّقِهِ الخاص بحقيقته لجميع الممكناتِ ، ولهذا أشرنا بقولنا : ( يباين سواه ضرورة ) .
(١) وهما هنا : اجتماع متعلقين على متعلَّق واحد
(٢) وهذا راجع لكل من تحصيل الحاصل واجتماع المثلين
.
(٣) والثاني هو مذهب الجمهور القائلين بتباين صفتي السمع والبصر عن العلم .
وعليهما تقاس الإدراكات الثلاثة الباقية على القول بها
The obtained (al-hāṣil), or the conjunction of two likes (ijtimāʿ al-mithlayn) (1): either what is attached to it is other than what knowledge is attached to, or the concealment of some known things from knowledge if what those perceptions are attached to is not attached to knowledge (2), and both matters are impossible.
I say: we choose the first of the two divisions; that what those perceptions are attached to is exactly what knowledge is attached to, and it does not entail from that obtaining the obtained nor conjunction of two likes; that is because these perceptions, since they are not unified in reality, whether we say they are types of knowledge or not (3), their attachments are likewise not unified, so the conjunction of their attachments in one attached object is not from obtaining the obtained, nor from conjunction of likes, but rather each attachment has a reality of disclosure (inkishāf) specific to it, not identical to the reality of another, and each reality is general for what is valid for it.
And this is as preceded: that the attached object of power and will is one—namely contingent things—and there does not follow from their conjunction in one attached object obtaining the obtained, due to the difference of the realities of their two attachments, and each of them is general with its specific attachment by its reality for all contingent things, and to this we pointed by our saying: 'it necessarily differs from other.'
(1) They are here: conjunction of two attachments upon one attached object.
(2) And this refers to both obtaining the obtained and conjunction of two likes.
(3) And the second is the position of the majority who hold the distinctness of the attributes of hearing and sight from knowledge. And upon them are measured the remaining three perceptions for those who hold them.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a potential objection regarding multiple perceptions (hearing, sight, etc.) attaching to the same object as knowledge. The author first states two possible scenarios for what happens when perceptions and knowledge attach to an object: either they attach to something other than what knowledge attaches to, or they attach to the same thing but knowledge misses some aspect—both impossible. He chooses the first possibility and argues that even when multiple perceptions attach to the same object as knowledge, this does not entail 'تحصيل الحاصل' (obtaining what is already obtained) or 'اجتماع المثلين' (conjunction of two likes). The reason is that each perception has a distinct reality of disclosure (انكشاف), so their attachments are not identical even if the object is one. He analogizes with the attributes of power (قدرة) and will (إرادة), which both attach to contingent things (ممكنات) yet differ in the reality of their attachment. The footnotes clarify: (1) defines اجتماع المثلين here as two attachments on one object; (2) says the scenario applies to both concepts; (3) notes that the second position (that perceptions are distinct from knowledge) is held by the majority, and that the remaining three perceptions (if held) are measured on the same principle.

Key Terms
تحصيل الحاصل
اجتماع المثلين
تعلق
انكشاف
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If multiple perceptions (hearing, sight, etc.) attach to the same object as knowledge, they are redundant (تحصيل الحاصل) or contradictory (اجتماع المثلين).
Correction: They are not redundant because each perception has a distinct reality of disclosure, so their attachments are different even when the object is one.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does the attachment of multiple perceptions to the same object as knowledge not entail 'تحصيل الحاصل'?
A) Because knowledge is not actually present
B) Because each perception has a distinct reality of disclosure
C) Because the object is different for each
D) Because perceptions are identical to knowledge

Key Takeaway
Multiple perceptions attach to the same object without redundancy because each has a distinct reality of disclosure, just as power and will differ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's attributes are distinct and non-redundant: each perception discloses reality in a unique way. Are you ever tempted to think that God’s knowledge of your flaws makes your repentance redundant? How does the distinct disclosure of hearing and seeing give you confidence that He truly comprehends the depth of your return to Him? Will you call upon Him today specifically as the All-Hearing and All-Seeing, trusting that these are not the same as His mere knowledge?
?
If every divine perception has its own 'reality of disclosure,' does that mean God knows your thoughts not just as information but in a way that is unique to each attribute? How does this truth affect the way you speak to Him in prayer—do you fully believe that He hears your voice as something different from merely knowing your words? Commit to praying with the certainty that your words reach a distinct attribute of hearing.
?
Sanusi uses the analogy of power and will to show that attributes sharing an object are not redundant. In your own life, do you sometimes assume that God's mercy and justice conflict, or that His knowledge and power are the same? How can reflecting on the distinctness of attributes deepen your awe and reliance on Him? Take a moment to thank Him for one specific attribute that you feel you need most right now.

Why Sight Cannot Be Stronger Than Knowledge in God
Sanusi explains why 'sight stronger than knowledge' is only for contingent beings, not God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the idea that sight is stronger than knowledge is only valid for contingent beings and not for God, because God's knowledge encompasses all things in detail.

Core Idea
God's knowledge already includes everything, so perception adds nothing new.

This lesson tackles a subtle objection: if sight is stronger than knowledge, then attributing perception to God might imply that He gains new information. Sanusi clarifies that this reasoning applies only to creatures with limited knowledge, not to the All-Knowing Creator. The passage also shows how we affirm God's attributes of hearing and sight based on revelation while maintaining His perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the footnotes as they clarify key points.
2
Identify the implicit objection
Notice that Sanusi is answering an implied question: How can perceptions attach to the same objects as knowledge when sight is stronger?
This objection arises from analogy with human experience.
3
Understand the contingent case
See why the claim 'sight is stronger than knowledge' holds for contingent beings: their knowledge is deficient, so perception can reveal unknown details.
4
Apply to God
Grasp why this claim is impossible for God: His knowledge necessarily encompasses all things in their totality and detail, leaving nothing for perception to disclose.
The key is God's absolute encompassment (al-iḥāṭa).
5
Affirm the attributes by revelation
Note that Sanusi concludes by affirming that hearing and sight are attributes of God based on revelation, and we accept them without fully grasping their reality.
This shows how reason and revelation work together in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك في حق
وما ثبت أنَّ المشاهدة أقوى مِنَ العلم إنَّما يصح الحادثِ (١) ؛ لنقص عليه وعدم إحاطته(٢) فقد ينكشف له عندَ المشاهدة أمورٌ لم يتعلَّق بها علمُهُ أصلاً ، أو تعلَّق لكن على سبيل الإجمال لا على سبيل التفصيل ، فيستفيد بسبب السمع والبصر علماً بما لم يكن معلوماً عندَه
وهذا مستحيل في حقِّهِ تعالى ؛ فإنَّ السمع والبصر لا ينكشفُ بهما في حقه تبارك وتعالى شيء لم يكن منكشفاً لعلمه جلَّ وعلا ؛ لوجوبِ
إحاطة علمه تبارك وتعالى بجميع المعلوماتِ ؛ جُمْلتِها وتفصيلها وإِنَّما السمع والبصر يزيدان على العلم في حقه جلَّ وعلا بحقيقتهما وتعلقهما الخاص بهما (٣) ، ولا يزيدان في حقيقة علمهِ تعالى شيئاً
